First Argument: Violation of Custom Leads to Absurdity
Razi argues that allowing violation of custom leads to absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that allowing violations of natural custom leads to absurd consequences, and why he concludes that miracles are impossible in this epistemic sense.

Core Idea
Violating custom would destroy all certainty about the world.

Razi begins his discussion on miracles with a preliminary argument. He contends that if we accept the possibility of violations of natural custom (such as the sudden creation of an old man without parents), then we can never be certain of anything we observe. This leads to an absurdity, which, he argues, proves that such violations are impossible. But is this argument conclusive? Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the concise style of argumentation.
Razi uses a hypothetical example to illustrate his point.
2
Identify the hypothetical
Look for the example of the old man created without parents. Understand why it is a violation of custom.
3
Follow the consequence
Trace how Razi applies this to observing 'Zayd' and the resulting uncertainty. Why does he call it absurd?
4
Grasp the conclusion
Understand the conclusion that violation of custom is impossible. Consider what this implies for miracles.
This is a preliminary argument; later discussions will address objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثلاثون الساعة شيخ من غير أب ولا أم ، ولكنا إذا شاهدنا زيدا لم نأمن من أن يكون الله خلق في هذه الساعة شيخًا من غير أب ولا أم مثل "زيد" في صورته وشكله وتخطيطه، ومعلوم أن تجويزه هذه الأشياء يفضي على الجهالات، ولما كان ذلك باطلا علمنا أن انخراق العادات غير ممكن.
The Thirtieth Issue: At this hour, an old man [appears] without father or mother. But if we saw Zayd, we would not be safe from [the possibility] that God created at this hour an old man without father or mother like Zayd in his image, form, and outline. And it is known that allowing these things leads to absurdities. Since that is false, we know that violation of customs (inkhirāq al-‘ādāt) is not possible.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage presents the Thirtieth Issue on miracles. Razi argues that if we allow violations of natural custom (inkhirāq al-‘ādāt), we would face absurd consequences. For example, if it were possible for God to create an old man instantly without parents, then when we see a person like Zayd, we could never be certain that he was not a newly created being who just appeared. This would destroy all certainty about the world. Since such absurdity is impossible, we conclude that violation of custom is impossible. This is a preliminary argument against the possibility of miracles, but the discussion will continue with objections.

Key Terms
انخراق العادات
جهالات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi's argument denies God's omnipotence.
Correction: Razi is not denying that God can do anything; he is arguing that if violations of custom were possible, we would have no epistemic certainty – that is an absurd consequence, so such violations are not possible in the order of knowledge.

Checkpoint
What is the absurd consequence of allowing violation of custom according to Razi?
A) People would have no parents.
B) We could never be sure that a person we see is not a newly created being.
C) God would be unjust.
D) Miracles would become widespread.

Key Takeaway
Razi's argument shows that allowing violations of natural custom would undermine all certainty about observed reality, thus he concludes they are impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the world around you, how often do you rely on the regularity of nature without thanking the One who upholds it? What would happen to your certainty about Allah's attributes if you allowed even a single break in custom? How would that affect your daily reliance on Him?
?
Razi argues that allowing one violation leads to total uncertainty. In your own life, where have you allowed one exception to a divine command that then led to a cascade of compromises? What would it take to return to firm certainty?
?
If you truly believed that at any moment Allah could replace the world with something utterly different, would your attachment to this life remain? Does your attachment to worldly stability reveal a hidden belief that custom is independent of Allah's will?

Second Objection: Miracles from the Prophet's Own Nature
Razi presents the second objection: miracles could be natural results of the prophet's unique soul or body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first aspect of the second objection against the divine origin of miracles, which argues that the prophet's own soul or bodily composition could be the source of his extraordinary abilities.

Core Idea
Miracles might arise from the prophet's own nature, not directly from God.

Razi now presents the second objection to the claim that miracles are created by God. The opponent concedes that violations of custom are possible but questions why they must be attributed to divine action. The first aspect of this objection explores two alternative explanations: either the prophet's rational soul is essentially superior, or his unique bodily temperament grants him special powers. This forces us to reconsider whether miracles necessarily prove divine intervention.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the two cases
The passage presents two scenarios: either we affirm the rational soul or we do not. Find how each scenario explains the prophet's ability to perform miracles.
The first case uses the soul's essence, the second uses bodily temperament.
3
Understand the conclusion
Notice that in both cases, the opponent concludes that we cannot know for sure that the miracle is from God. This challenges the standard proof of prophethood.
4
Reflect on the rhetorical question
Consider the question: 'Do you not see that each human has a specific temperament...?' This is used to make the alternative explanation seem plausible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتراض الثاني: سلمنا أن انخراق العادات غير ممتنع إلا أنا نقول : لم قلتم : إن هذا حصل بإيجاد الله وتقرير هذا السؤال من وجوه : الأول: نحن بين أمرين إما أن نثبت النفس الناطقة البشرية، وإما أنا لا نثبتها فإن أثبتنا النفس الناطقة، فلم لا يجوز أن يقا إن نفس النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم مخالفة لسائر النفوس بالماهية والحقيقة، فهي لماهيتها المخصوصة وحقيقتها التي باعتبارها امتازت عن سائر الحقائق، قدرت على ما لا يقدر عليه غيره، وأما إن قلنا : إن القول بالنفوس الناطقة باطل، فلا بد أن نعترف أن لكل واحد من أشخاص الناس مزاجا مخصوصا وتركيبا ،مخصوصاً به امتاز عن سائر الأشخاص، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال: به امتاز هذا الشخص عن سائر الأشخاص علة لكونه قادرًا على الأفعال التي يعجز ما عنها غيره؟ ألا ترى أن لكل إنسان مزاجًا مخصوصا، وذلك المزاج يقتضي في ظاهره خلقاً، وفي باطنه خلقا لا يحصلان البتة في غيره، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال: تلك الخصوصية تكون مبدأ لتلك القدرة المخصوصة ؟ وعلى هذا التقدير لا يمكننا أن نعلم أن هذه المعجز هو فعل الله تعالى.
The Second Objection: We concede that violation of custom is not impossible, but we ask: Why do you say that this [miracle] occurred by God's creation? And the formulation of this question has aspects: First: We have two cases: either we affirm the rational soul (al-nafs al-nāṭiqa) of humans, or we do not affirm it. If we affirm the rational soul, why is it not permissible to say that the soul of the Prophet (peace be upon him) is different from other souls in essence and reality, and due to its specific essence and reality by which it is distinguished from all other realities, it is able to do what others cannot? And if we say that the doctrine of rational souls is false, then we must admit that each human individual has a specific temperament (mizāj) and composition by which he is distinguished from other individuals. Why then is it not permissible to say that this individual's distinctness from other individuals is the cause of his being able to perform acts that others cannot? Do you not see that each human has a specific temperament, and that temperament demands in its outward appearance a character and in its inward a character that are absolutely not found in anyone else? Why then is it not permissible to say that that specificity is the source (mabda’) of that specific power? On this assumption, we cannot know that this miracle is an act of God Almighty.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the Second Objection against the divine origin of miracles. The opponent concedes that violation of custom is possible but questions why they must be attributed to God. The first aspect (al-wajh al-awwal) considers two possibilities: either we affirm the existence of a rational soul (al-nafs al-nāṭiqa) or we do not. If we affirm it, then the prophet's soul could be essentially different and thus naturally powerful. If we reject the rational soul, then each person's unique bodily temperament (mizāj) could be the source of special abilities. In either case, the prophet's miracles could be due to his own nature, not directly from God. Thus, we cannot be certain that miracles are divine acts. Razi presents this as a coherent objection that must be refuted.

Key Terms
النفس الناطقة
المزاج
التركيب
الخصوصية
المعجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This objection denies that miracles happen at all.
Correction: The objection does not deny miracles; it offers alternative natural explanations that do not require direct divine intervention.

Checkpoint
What is the first aspect of the second objection?
A) The prophet's special diet causes miracles.
B) Either the prophet's soul or his bodily temperament can explain his ability to perform miracles.
C) Miracles are impossible.
D) God's power is limited.

Key Takeaway
The second objection challenges certainty that miracles are divine by offering natural explanations rooted in the prophet's own soul or body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you attribute a miracle to God, are you certain it is directly from Him, or could it be that the person's nature made it possible? How does this uncertainty affect your reliance on miracles as proofs of prophethood?
?
If the Prophet's soul were essentially different, would that diminish your conviction that his message is from God? What does your answer reveal about where you place your trust?
?
The opponent presents two alternative explanations for miracles. Which of these two (soul or temperament) feels more unsettling to you, and why? Are you willing to examine the reasons for your discomfort?

Second Face: Miracles from a Special Drug
Razi presents the second face of the objection: miracles might result from a special drug.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection to miracles being divine: that a special drug with extraordinary properties could produce miraculous effects, and why this challenges the certainty of divine origin.

Core Idea
Even if not from the prophet, a special drug could explain miracles.

This lesson presents the second face of the objection against using miracles as proof of prophethood. The opponent grants that miracles are not from the prophet's extraordinary soul or body, but suggests they could be from a drug with special properties. We will examine this argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
2
Identify the opponent's reasoning
Notice how the opponent uses an analogy with known medicines that increase intelligence or strength to suggest that a special drug could produce miracles.
The key analogy is that if ordinary drugs can have amazing effects, why not a drug with special properties?
3
Consider the conclusion
The opponent concludes that if this is possible, we cannot be certain that a miracle is an act of God. Think about why this undermines the proof of prophethood.
This is a skeptical challenge: it introduces a naturalistic alternative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني : في تقرير هذا المقام هب أن تلك المعجزات لم تحصل لأجل خصوصية نفسه أو خصوصية بدنه، لكن لا شك أن أنواع الأدوية كثيرة، والآثار الصادرة عنها عجيبة كثيرة، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن هذا المدعى وجد دواء له قوة وخاصية تقتضي ظهور تلك الآثار والمعجزات عند تناول ذلك الدواء ؟ ألا ترى أن الناس يقولون : إن أكل الدواء الفلاني يزيد في الذكاء، وأكل الدواء الآخر يزيد في القوة الفلانية، فلم لا يجوز وجود دواء آخر يزيد في قوة الإدراك والعقل زيادة منتهية إلى حد هذا الاحتمال لا يمكن القطع بأن هذا المعجز فعل الله تعالى؟
The Second Face: In formulating this position, grant that those miracles did not occur due to the specificity of his soul or the specificity of his body, but there is no doubt that the types of medicines are many, and the effects issuing from them are amazing and many. So why is it not permissible to say that this claimant found a medicine that has a power and property that necessitates the appearance of those effects and miracles when that medicine is taken? Do you not see that people say: Eating such-and-such a medicine increases intelligence, and eating another medicine increases such-and-such strength. So why is it not permissible that there exists another medicine that increases the power of perception and intellect to the point that this possibility leads to the inability to be certain that this miracle is an act of God Almighty?
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This is the second face (al-wajh al-thānī) of the second objection. The opponent suggests that even if we rule out the prophet's soul or body as the cause, miracles could still be explained by a special drug with extraordinary properties. He notes that we already know medicines can produce amazing effects, such as increasing intelligence or strength. So it is possible that a medicine exists that can induce miraculous cognitive or physical abilities. If that is possible, we cannot be certain that a given miracle is divine.

Key Terms
دواء
خاصية
قوة الإدراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument is about intoxicating or hallucinogenic drugs that cause visions.
Correction: The second face does not refer to intoxicants but to hypothetical substances with specific properties that enhance cognitive or physical abilities, similar to known medicines that increase intelligence or strength.

Checkpoint
What does the second face propose as an alternative explanation for miracles?
A) The prophet's diet
B) A special drug or potion
C) The prophet's education
D) Genetic mutation

Key Takeaway
The second face challenges the certainty that miracles are divine by suggesting a naturalistic explanation via a special drug, showing the need for stronger proofs of prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever attributed an extraordinary event directly to God when a natural explanation was possible? How does this objection challenge you to examine the basis of your certainty?
?
If someone were to present a plausible natural cause for a miracle you deeply believe in, would your faith waver? What does that reveal about the foundation of your belief?
?
This objection forces you to consider whether your faith rests on proof that leaves no room for doubt. Are you willing to confront the possibility that your certainty might be shaken, and if so, what will you anchor yourself to?

The Third Face: Miracles from Jinn and Devils
Razi presents the third face and answers two objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi defends the possibility that miracles could be assisted by jinn and devils, and how he answers two key objections.

Core Idea
Mere possibility, not certainty, is enough to challenge the proof of prophethood.

This passage presents the third face of a skeptical argument against miracles as proof of prophethood. The opponent suggests that jinn or devils could be the true source of extraordinary events. Razi then anticipates two natural objections and provides responses, showing that the possibility cannot be dismissed by reliance on prophetic reports alone. We will examine the reasoning and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argumentative flow come through before you interpret.
Notice that this is a structured objection-and-answer format.
2
Identify the two objections and Razi's answers
The passage begins with the third face (objection), then presents two sub-objections, then answers each. Mark them clearly in the text.
The first objection is about cursing; the second is about circularity.
3
Understand the role of mere possibility
Razi's second answer hinges on 'mere possibility' (الاحتمال). Reflect on why he says the challenger does not need to affirm the existence of jinn definitively.
This is a key epistemological move in kalam argumentation.
4
Reflect on the broader debate
Consider how this argument fits into the overall discussion of miracles as proof of prophethood. Does Razi think this objection defeats the proof? Or does he merely present it as a challenge?
Hint: The passage is part of a larger work on 'the forty' principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : ظهور هذه المعجزات إنما كان بإعانة الجن والشياطين، وذلك لأنا وإن كنا نعرف مقادير عقول البشر، وقدرهم، إلا أنا لا نعرف مقادير عقول الشياطين وقدرهم فلعل هذه المعجزات حصلت بإعانة الشياطين، ولعل الإخبار عن الغيوب حصل بإلقاء الشياطين تلك الغيوب إليهم. لا يقال : هذا باطل من وجهين : الأول : أن الأنبياء - صلوات الله وسلمه عليهم - دعوا الخلق إلى لعن الشياطين، فكيف يليق بالشياطين أن يعينوهم ؟ والثاني: أنا إنما نثبت الجن والشياطين بإخبار الأنبياء - صلوات الله وسلمه عليهم - فلو جعلنا القول بالجن والشياطين طاعنا في النبوة كنا قد أبطلنا الأصل بالفرع، وذلك باطل. لأنا نجيب عن الأول بأن الشيطان إذا كان مشغوفًا بإضلال الخلق - وهذا الطريق يفضي إلى حصول مقصوده - لم يبعد أن يرضى بذلك اللعن ليحصل هذا المقصود له. ونحيب عن الثاني : أنا لا نحتاج في مقام السؤال إلى الجزم بوجود الجن والشياطين، بل يكفينا مجرد الاحتمال ومجرد الاحتمال لا يتوقف القول بصحة النبوة.
The Third Face: Why is it not permissible to say that the occurrence of these miracles was only through the assistance of the jinn and the devils? Because, although we know the capacities of human intellects and powers, we do not know the capacities of the intellects and powers of the devils. Perhaps these miracles occurred through the assistance of the devils, and perhaps the reports of the unseen occurred through the devils casting those unseen things to them. It should not be said: This is false for two reasons. First: The prophets (peace and blessings be upon them) called people to curse the devils, so how can it be fitting for the devils to assist them? Second: We only affirm the existence of jinn and devils through the reports of the prophets (peace and blessings be upon them). If we make the statement about jinn and devils a criticism of prophethood, then we have invalidated the principle by the branch, and that is false. For we answer the first [objection] as follows: If the devil is devoted to leading people astray, and this way leads to the attainment of his goal, it is not far-fetched that he would accept that cursing in order to achieve this goal of his. And we answer the second: In the context of the question, we do not need to affirm the existence of jinn and devils definitively; rather, mere possibility suffices for us, and mere possibility is not conditional upon the validity of prophethood.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage presents the third face (al-wajh al-thālith) of the objection: miracles might be due to the assistance of jinn and devils. Since we do not know the full capabilities of jinn, they may be able to produce wonders. Additionally, news of the unseen might come from them. The author then anticipates two objections: (1) prophets cursed devils, so why would devils help them? (2) We only know of jinn through prophets, so using jinn to doubt prophets is circular. Razi answers the first by noting that the devil might tolerate cursing if his ultimate aim of misguidance is served. He answers the second by explaining that the challenger does not need to assert the existence of jinn with certainty; a mere possibility of their existence is sufficient to cast doubt. This shows that the opponent's argument is not refuted by pointing to reliance on prophetic reports.

Key Terms
الجن
الشياطين
إعانة
الاحتمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument requires affirming the existence of jinn and devils.
Correction: The argument only requires the mere possibility of their existence to cast doubt.

Checkpoint
How does Razi answer the objection that prophets cursed devils, so devils would not help them?
A) Devils are ignorant of the curses.
B) Devils may accept cursing to achieve their goal of misguidance.
C) Prophets do not truly curse devils.
D) Devils have no choice.

Key Takeaway
The mere possibility of jinn assistance undermines the conclusiveness of miracles as proof, and this challenge is not refuted by pointing to reliance on prophetic reports.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a doubt about your faith that you cannot fully refute, do you dismiss it as irrelevant or do you honestly confront the possibility it raises? How does your reliance on 'mere possibility' in this lesson expose the difference between certainty and comfort in your own belief?
?
Razi shows that a proof can be challenged without proving the alternative. Is there an area of your life where you have built certainty on a proof that, upon reflection, might be vulnerable to a mere possibility you have never allowed yourself to consider? Will you investigate that possibility today?
?
If you knew that a single unproven possibility could shake the foundation of your religion's truth claims, would that alarm you or liberate you? This lesson invites you to trust not in the absence of doubt but in the strength of faith despite doubt. Do you trust God enough to sit with unanswered questions?

The First Objection: Miracles as the Start of a New Habit
Razi presents the first objection: a miracle may be the initial occurrence of a new habitual pattern.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection that a miracle might simply be the first occurrence of a new natural habit, rather than a confirmation of prophethood.

Core Idea
A miracle could be the first link in a new chain of nature.

Imam Razi begins his list of additional objections with a fundamental point: if the world is created and habitual events must have a first occurrence, then why can a miracle not be simply the beginning of a new custom? This objection challenges the assumption that miracles are unique confirmations, suggesting they could be the start of a recurring pattern.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage for the first objection.
Read the text carefully to understand the analogy.
2
Identify the key premise.
The world is originated, so every habitual act has a first occurrence.
This is the foundation of the objection.
3
Follow the conclusion.
A miracle could be that first occurrence, later becoming a habit.
4
Reflect on the challenge.
Consider why this undermines the argument that miracles confirm prophecy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنا نذكر وجوها أخر على سبيل التبرع : الأول : أن جملة هذه الأفعال الموافقة للعادات لا بد أن يكون لها أول لما ثبت أن العالم محدث، وحدوث كل واحد منها في المرة الأولى يكون على خلاف العادة، ثم إنه تعالى جعل ذلك دائمًا مستمراً فيما بعد ذلك، فكذا ههنا لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه سبحانه وتعالى أراد أن يبدأ بإحداث هذا الفعل الخارق للعادة ثم إنه يديمه بعد ذلك ويجعله عادة مستمرة بعد ذلك؟ وبالجملة فلم لا يجوز أن يكون هذا الذي حدث هو ابتداء عادة وستصير مستمرة بعد ذلك ؟ الثاني : أنه يجوز أن يكون حدوث هذا الشيء على سبيل العادة إلا أنه عادة لا تحصل إلا في أزمنة متطاولة، وذلك لأن دور الفلك الثامن لا يتم - عند بعضهم - إلا في مدة سنة وثلاثين ألف سنة، وعلى هذا تكون العادة المستمرة المطردة هو أن كل كوكب يحصل في كل ستة وثلاثين ألف سنة في موضع من الفلك، فمن نظر إلى حصوله في ذلك الوقت ظن أنه خارق للعادة ومن عرف الوضع المقتضي لذلك علم أنه عادة متطاولة، فكذا ههنا يجوز أن يقال : هذا الذي حدث إنما حدث على وفق عادة متطاولة. الثالث : أنه لما انشق القمر على السماء فلعل إنسانا أخر في جانب آخر من الأرض ادعى النبوة وطلب من الله تعالى إظهار المعجزة في هذا الوقت، وهو تعالى إنما أظهره في ذلك الوقت تصديقا لذلك الإنسان لا لهذا الإنسان. الرابع : أن هذا الإنسان لما ادعى الرسالة على سبيل الكذب، وطلب من الله تعالى إظهار المعجزة فإن الله يظهر المعجزة على وفق دعواه حتى يصير موهما لكونه صادقًا، إلا انه لما قرر في عقل المكلف أنه يجوز أن يكون غرض الله تعالى منه شيئًا آخر سوى التصديق وكان خلق هذا المعجز يجري مجرى إنزال المتشابهات، وخلق الأوهام المورثة للشبهات، وكان المقصود منها أنه الاحتراز عنها، فإذا احترز المكلف عنها مع صعوبة الاحتراز عنها، كان ثوابه أعظم. وعلى الجملة فمن الذي يمكنه أن يعرف أقسام حكم الله تعالى في خلق الأشياء ؟ وحينئذ كان الجزم بأنه لا غرض لله تعالى في هذا الفعل إلا هذا الوجه الواحد تحكما من غير دليل، والله الهادي. الاعتراض الخامس : هب أن غرضه تعالى من خلق هذه المعجزات، أن يستدل به على أن الله صدقه فلم قلتم : إن هذا الاستدلال حق؟ بيانه أن على مذهب أهل السنة الله تعالى خالق الكفر في قلب الكافر، وخلق ما يوهم الكفر ليس أبعد منه وعلى هذا التقدير لا يمكن الاستدلال بالمعجزة على كون المدعي صادقًا. فهذا مجموع الاعتراضات في هذا المقام.
Then we mention other points as a bonus: First: That the totality of these habitual actions must have a first occurrence, since it is established that the world is originated, and the occurrence of each one of them the first time is contrary to habit, then He, exalted, made it perpetual afterward. So likewise here, why is it not permissible to say: He, glorified and exalted, intended to initiate this extraordinary act then perpetuate it and make it a continuous custom afterward? In short, why is it not permissible that what occurred is the beginning of a custom that will become continuous afterward? Second: That it is permissible that the occurrence of this thing is by way of habit, but a habit that only occurs in long periods. For the revolution of the eighth sphere—according to some—is only completed in a period of thirty-six thousand years. On this basis, the continuous regular habit is that each planet occurs every thirty-six thousand years in a position of the sphere. So whoever looks at its occurrence at that time thinks it is extraordinary, but whoever knows the situation necessitating that knows that it is a long-term habit. So likewise here, it is permissible to say: What occurred occurred only according to a long-term habit. Third: That when the moon split in the sky, perhaps another person in another part of the earth claimed prophethood and asked God Almighty to show a miracle at that time, and He only showed it at that time as a confirmation of that person, not of this person. Fourth: That when this person claimed the message falsely and asked God Almighty to show a miracle, God shows the miracle according to his claim so that it becomes suggestive that he is truthful. But since He established in the mind of the responsible person that it is permissible that God's purpose in it is something other than confirmation, and the creation of this miracle is like the sending down of ambiguous verses and the creation of illusions that cause doubts, and the purpose of them is that he be cautious about them. So when the responsible person is cautious about them despite the difficulty of caution, his reward is greater. In short, who can know the categories of God's wisdom in creating things? Then to assert positively that there is no purpose for God in this act except this single aspect is arbitrary without evidence. And God is the Guide. The Fifth Objection: Suppose His purpose in creating these miracles is that inference be made by them that God confirmed him. Why do you say that this inference is valid? Its explanation is that according to the doctrine of the Ahl al-Sunnah, God creates disbelief in the heart of the disbeliever, and creating what suggests disbelief is not more remote than that. On this basis, inference by the miracle that the claimant is truthful is not possible. So this is the total of the objections in this place.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage presents five additional objections raised by deniers against the argument that miracles indicate the truthfulness of a claimant to prophethood. The first objection suggests that a miracle might simply be the beginning of a new habit (custom) in nature, as the world is originated and habitual events must have a first occurrence. The second objection proposes that a miracle might be the result of a very long-term astronomical cycle, making it appear extraordinary to those unaware of its periodicity. The third objection speculates that the same supernatural event (like the splitting of the moon) could be a response to another claimant elsewhere, not necessarily the one before us. The fourth objection argues that God might allow a false claimant to perform a miracle to test believers, making it ambiguous like the ambiguous verses of the Quran. The fifth objection points out that on Sunni doctrine, God creates disbelief in the disbelievers, so a miracle could be created as a trial rather than a confirmation. The passage concludes by stating that these are the collected objections on this topic.

Key Terms
العادة
الخارق للعادة
الحدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People think that a miraculous event must be a direct confirmation of a prophet's truthfulness.
Correction: The first objection shows that the event could simply be the start of a new natural pattern, not necessarily tied to the prophet's claim.

Checkpoint
What is the main premise of the first objection?
A) The world is eternal, so habits never change.
B) The world is created, so every habitual action has a first occurrence.
C) Miracles are always repeated.
D) God only creates miracles for prophets.

Key Takeaway
The first objection argues that a miracle could simply be the beginning of a new natural habit, not a unique divine sign.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If a miracle could be just the start of a new natural habit, how does that affect your trust in the miracles reported in the Quran? Is your faith dependent on them being singular interruptions of nature, or can it withstand the possibility that they were the first in a new custom? Probe your heart: do you need miracles to be 'supernatural' to believe, or is your certainty based on something deeper?
?
This objection suggests that even extraordinary events might not be direct divine signs. Have you ever assumed that an unusual event in your life was a direct sign from God? How do you discern whether it is a sign or just a new natural pattern? What does this teach you about your own desire for certainty?
?
The objection is based on the idea that all habits have a beginning. Consider your own habits—spiritual or otherwise—that you consider 'natural' or 'inevitable.' Are you open to the possibility that God could establish new 'habits' in your life that break your current patterns? What would that require of you?

The Second Objection: Miracles as Long-Term Astronomical Cycles
Razi presents the second objection: miracles may be due to long-term cycles not commonly known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection that extraordinary events might be due to very long-term cycles that appear miraculous to those unaware of their periodicity.

Core Idea
What seems miraculous might be a rare but natural cycle.

The second objection draws on astronomical cycles: the revolution of the eighth sphere takes thirty-six thousand years. An event that occurs only once in such a long period would appear extraordinary to someone unaware of the cycle. Razi applies this analogy to prophetic miracles, suggesting they might be the result of similarly long-term natural patterns.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage for the second objection.
Read carefully to understand the astronomical analogy.
2
Understand the analogy.
The eighth sphere's revolution is a very long period.
36,000 years.
3
Notice the conclusion.
The event could be a long-term habit.
4
Consider the epistemological challenge.
Only those aware of the cycle know it's habitual.

Source Text

?

The Third Objection: The Miracle Might Be for Another Claimant
Razi presents the third objection: a miracle may be a response to a different claimant, not the one observed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection that a single supernatural event, like the splitting of the moon, could be a response to a different claimant elsewhere.

Core Idea
One miracle, two claims: which prophet is it for?

The third objection introduces the possibility that a miracle reported during one prophet's claim might actually be a confirmation of another claimant in a different location. For example, the splitting of the moon is attributed to Prophet Muhammad, but Razi suggests it might have been in response to another person's prayer. This raises the issue of attribution.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage for the third objection.
Read the short passage carefully.
2
Note the scenario.
The moon splitting could be for another claimant.
3
Understand the implication.
We cannot automatically attribute the miracle to the prophet in front of us.
4
Reflect on the difficulty.
Consider how to verify the intended recipient of a miracle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنا نذكر وجوها أخر على سبيل التبرع : الأول : أن جملة هذه الأفعال الموافقة للعادات لا بد أن يكون لها أول لما ثبت أن العالم محدث، وحدوث كل واحد منها في المرة الأولى يكون على خلاف العادة، ثم إنه تعالى جعل ذلك دائمًا مستمراً فيما بعد ذلك، فكذا ههنا لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه سبحانه وتعالى أراد أن يبدأ بإحداث هذا الفعل الخارق للعادة ثم إنه يديمه بعد ذلك ويجعله عادة مستمرة بعد ذلك؟ وبالجملة فلم لا يجوز أن يكون هذا الذي حدث هو ابتداء عادة وستصير مستمرة بعد ذلك ؟ الثاني : أنه يجوز أن يكون حدوث هذا الشيء على سبيل العادة إلا أنه عادة لا تحصل إلا في أزمنة متطاولة، وذلك لأن دور الفلك الثامن لا يتم - عند بعضهم - إلا في مدة سنة وثلاثين ألف سنة، وعلى هذا تكون العادة المستمرة المطردة هو أن كل كوكب يحصل في كل ستة وثلاثين ألف سنة في موضع من الفلك، فمن نظر إلى حصوله في ذلك الوقت ظن أنه خارق للعادة ومن عرف الوضع المقتضي لذلك علم أنه عادة متطاولة، فكذا ههنا يجوز أن يقال : هذا الذي حدث إنما حدث على وفق عادة متطاولة. الثالث : أنه لما انشق القمر على السماء فلعل إنسانا أخر في جانب آخر من الأرض ادعى النبوة وطلب من الله تعالى إظهار المعجزة في هذا الوقت، وهو تعالى إنما أظهره في ذلك الوقت تصديقا لذلك الإنسان لا لهذا الإنسان. الرابع : أن هذا الإنسان لما ادعى الرسالة على سبيل الكذب، وطلب من الله تعالى إظهار المعجزة فإن الله يظهر المعجزة على وفق دعواه حتى يصير موهما لكونه صادقًا، إلا انه لما قرر في عقل المكلف أنه يجوز أن يكون غرض الله تعالى منه شيئًا آخر سوى التصديق وكان خلق هذا المعجز يجري مجرى إنزال المتشابهات، وخلق الأوهام المورثة للشبهات، وكان المقصود منها أنه الاحتراز عنها، فإذا احترز المكلف عنها مع صعوبة الاحتراز عنها، كان ثوابه أعظم. وعلى الجملة فمن الذي يمكنه أن يعرف أقسام حكم الله تعالى في خلق الأشياء ؟ وحينئذ كان الجزم بأنه لا غرض لله تعالى في هذا الفعل إلا هذا الوجه الواحد تحكما من غير دليل، والله الهادي. الاعتراض الخامس : هب أن غرضه تعالى من خلق هذه المعجزات، أن يستدل به على أن الله صدقه فلم قلتم : إن هذا الاستدلال حق؟ بيانه أن على مذهب أهل السنة الله تعالى خالق الكفر في قلب الكافر، وخلق ما يوهم الكفر ليس أبعد منه وعلى هذا التقدير لا يمكن الاستدلال بالمعجزة على كون المدعي صادقًا. فهذا مجموع الاعتراضات في هذا المقام.
Then we mention other points as a bonus: First: That the totality of these habitual actions must have a first occurrence, since it is established that the world is originated, and the occurrence of each one of them the first time is contrary to habit, then He, exalted, made it perpetual afterward. So likewise here, why is it not permissible to say: He, glorified and exalted, intended to initiate this extraordinary act then perpetuate it and make it a continuous custom afterward? In short, why is it not permissible that what occurred is the beginning of a custom that will become continuous afterward? Second: That it is permissible that the occurrence of this thing is by way of habit, but a habit that only occurs in long periods. For the revolution of the eighth sphere—according to some—is only completed in a period of thirty-six thousand years. On this basis, the continuous regular habit is that each planet occurs every thirty-six thousand years in a position of the sphere. So whoever looks at its occurrence at that time thinks it is extraordinary, but whoever knows the situation necessitating that knows that it is a long-term habit. So likewise here, it is permissible to say: What occurred occurred only according to a long-term habit. Third: That when the moon split in the sky, perhaps another person in another part of the earth claimed prophethood and asked God Almighty to show a miracle at that time, and He only showed it at that time as a confirmation of that person, not of this person. Fourth: That when this person claimed the message falsely and asked God Almighty to show a miracle, God shows the miracle according to his claim so that it becomes suggestive that he is truthful. But since He established in the mind of the responsible person that it is permissible that God's purpose in it is something other than confirmation, and the creation of this miracle is like the sending down of ambiguous verses and the creation of illusions that cause doubts, and the purpose of them is that he be cautious about them. So when the responsible person is cautious about them despite the difficulty of caution, his reward is greater. In short, who can know the categories of God's wisdom in creating things? Then to assert positively that there is no purpose for God in this act except this single aspect is arbitrary without evidence. And God is the Guide. The Fifth Objection: Suppose His purpose in creating these miracles is that inference be made by them that God confirmed him. Why do you say that this inference is valid? Its explanation is that according to the doctrine of the Ahl al-Sunnah, God creates disbelief in the heart of the disbeliever, and creating what suggests disbelief is not more remote than that. On this basis, inference by the miracle that the claimant is truthful is not possible. So this is the total of the objections in this place.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage presents five additional objections raised by deniers against the argument that miracles indicate the truthfulness of a claimant to prophethood. The first objection suggests that a miracle might simply be the beginning of a new habit (custom) in nature, as the world is originated and habitual events must have a first occurrence. The second objection proposes that a miracle might be the result of a very long-term astronomical cycle, making it appear extraordinary to those unaware of its periodicity. The third objection speculates that the same supernatural event (like the splitting of the moon) could be a response to another claimant elsewhere, not necessarily the one before us. The fourth objection argues that God might allow a false claimant to perform a miracle to test believers, making it ambiguous like the ambiguous verses of the Quran. The fifth objection points out that on Sunni doctrine, God creates disbelief in the disbelievers, so a miracle could be created as a trial rather than a confirmation. The passage concludes by stating that these are the collected objections on this topic.

Key Terms
انشقاق القمر
تصديق
النبوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a miracle occurs in the presence of a claimant, it must be for him.
Correction: It could be for another claimant elsewhere who also made a request.

Checkpoint
According to the third objection, the splitting of the moon might have been a confirmation for whom?
A) The prophet present at the time.
B) Another claimant in a different part of the world.
C) All prophets simultaneously.
D) The sun and moon deities.

Key Takeaway
The third objection warns that a miracle may be a response to a different claimant, so we cannot automatically attribute it to the prophet we are testing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a sign or blessing in your life, do you assume it is specifically for you, or could it be a response to someone else's plea? How does this objection challenge your certainty about who God is responding to?
?
If you were to witness a miracle today, would you be absolutely sure it was for a particular person? What if another person in another part of the world claimed the same miracle? How would you discern? This passage warns against hasty attribution. How can you apply this caution in your own judgments about God's actions?
?
Think of a time when you attributed a specific outcome to your own efforts or prayers. Could it have been that God was answering someone else's prayer through you? How does this realization humble you, and what will you change in how you interpret events in your life?

Miracles

The Fourth Objection: Miracles as Divine Tests and Illusions
Razi presents the fourth objection: miracles could be tests, like ambiguous verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection that God might create a miracle for a false claimant to test believers, similar to sending ambiguous verses (mutashabihat) and creating illusions.

Core Idea
A miracle could be a test, not a confirmation.

The fourth objection is more complex. It argues that God may grant a miracle to a false prophet as a trial, creating ambiguity to increase the reward for those who remain cautious. This is analogous to the ambiguous verses (mutashabihat) in the Quran, which are tests of faith. The objection emphasizes that we cannot know all the divine purposes behind a miracle.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage for the fourth objection.
Read carefully; it is longer.
2
Understand the scenario.
A false claimant asks for a miracle and God grants it.
3
Note the purpose.
To test believers by creating illusion and ambiguity.
4
Recognize the analogy.
Similar to ambiguous verses and the principle of caution.
5
Reflect on the conclusion.
We cannot definitively know God's purpose.

Misconception & Correction

In the fourth objection, what is the primary purpose of God creating illusions and ambiguous verses?
A) To confuse people.
B) To test believers and increase their reward for caution.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witnessed something that seemed to confirm a religious claim, did you ever stop to consider that God might be testing you rather than endorsing the claim? How does this lesson challenge your natural desire for clear signs? Will you now practice caution before accepting such appearances?
?
The passage says that the purpose of ambiguous verses and illusions is for you to be cautious—and that this caution is difficult but brings greater reward. In your own life, where is God asking you to exercise difficult caution right now? What is the 'ambiguous verse' or 'illusion' you are facing, and what would it mean for you to hold back from jumping to conclusions?
?

How would your attitude change if you truly accepted that divine wisdom may be beyond your grasp, and that your role is to remain cautious and obedient?

The Fifth Objection: Sunni Doctrine Makes Miracle Inference Invalid
Razi presents the fifth objection based on Ahl al-Sunna's doctrine of creation of disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth objection that, according to Sunni theology where God creates disbelief in the disbeliever's heart, a miracle cannot serve as a valid inference for truthfulness because God could also create the illusion of confirmation.

Core Idea
If God creates disbelief, He can also create the semblance of a miracle.

The fifth objection strikes at the heart of the argument by referencing the Sunni doctrine that God creates disbelief in the heart of the disbeliever (kasb theory). If God can create disbelief, He can also create a miracle that appears to confirm a false claim, thereby making the miracle an unreliable indicator. This objection undermines the assumption that miracles are trustworthy signs.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage for the fifth objection.
Read carefully; it is the final objection.
2
Understand the Sunni doctrine.
God creates disbelief in the disbeliever.
3
Apply the logic.
If God can create disbelief, He can also create a miracle that suggests truth.
4
Conclude.
Inference from miracle to truthfulness is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

This passage presents five additional objections raised by deniers against the argument that miracles indicate the truthfulness of a claimant to prophethood. The first objection suggests that a miracle might simply be the beginning of a new habit (custom) in nature, as the world is originated and habitual events must have a first occurrence. The second objection proposes that a miracle might be the result of a very long-term astronomical cycle, making it appear extraordinary to those unaware of its periodicity. The third objection speculates that the same supernatural event (like the splitting of the moon) could be a response to another claimant elsewhere, not necessarily the one before us. The fourth objection argues that God might allow a false claimant to perform a miracle to test believers, making it ambiguous like the ambiguous verses of the Quran. The fifth objection points out that on Sunni doctrine, God creates disbelief in the disbelievers, so a miracle could be created as a trial rather than a confirmation. The passage concludes by stating that these are the collected objections on this topic.

Key Terms
أهل السنة
الكفر
الاستدلال
خالق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles are objective proofs that cannot be manipulated by God.
Correction: In Sunni theology, God can create any effect, including a miracle that deceives, so miracles are not reliable indicators.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth objection, why is inference from a miracle invalid?
A) Miracles are not created by God.
B) God can create a miracle that suggests truth just as He creates disbelief.
C) The claimant might be lying.
D) The audience might not understand the miracle.

Key Takeaway
The fifth objection uses Sunni theology to argue that miracles cannot be valid proofs because God can create illusions of truth just as He creates disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face doubt about a matter of faith, do you demand a miraculous sign to confirm truth? How does Razi's objection expose the weakness of such a demand?
?
If God can create disbelief in a heart, what does that say about your own heart's reliance on external proofs? Are you truly submitting to God's sovereignty even when signs are ambiguous?
?
This objection suggests that even miracles can be deceptive if God wills. How does this affect your reliance on your own perceptions? Are you willing to trust God's word even when your senses might mislead you?

Correlation Does Not Imply Causation in Miracles
Deniers argue correlation fails to prove causality in miracle arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the deniers' argument that the mere coincidence of a miracle-like act with a claim does not prove that the act was caused by the claim.

Core Idea
Correlation does not imply causation, even for miracles.

The deniers challenge the assumption that an extraordinary act occurring at the time of a claim is a definitive divine confirmation. They accept that it may suggest such, but they insist that other possible causes—like a scorpion, a headache, or a gesture—undermine any claim to certainty. This lesson unpacks their first argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the deniers' first position
Read the Arabic passage for the first position and its translation. Notice how they frame the argument by questioning the logical leap from suggestion to certainty.
2
Identify the correlation argument
The deniers describe the believers' reasoning: the act occurred at the same time as the request, and not before, so correlation indicates causality. Understand this step.
3
Analyze the rejection of correlation
The deniers argue that correlation does not prove causality because another cause could have occurred at that same moment. Examine the three alternative causes they list: scorpion, headache, or another person's gesture.
Notice the specific examples they use to illustrate the point.
4
Reflect on the outcome
The conclusion is that the act only suggests (dhahir) confirmation, not certainty (qat'). Understand why they say 'no way to definitive certainty.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال المنكرون وأما ما عولتم عليه من الدلائل في بيان أن المعجزات تدل على الصدق فحاصل الكلام فيه أنكم ادعيتم أن في الشاهد إقدام الملك على الفعل الخارق للعادة يدل على كونه مصدقا للمدعى في دعواه، وإذا ثبت ذلك في الشاهد فحينئذ تقيسون الغائب عليه فلنبحث عن كل واحد من هذين المقامين : أما المقام الأول : فنقول : لا نسلم أن ظهور ذلك الفعل من الملك، يدل على أنه يصدق المدعي في دعواه ولا ننكر أنه يوهم ذلك، وأما أن يدل عليه قطعا فهذا ممنوع، وذلك لأن أقصى ما في الباب أن يقال: إنه حصل ذلك الفعل عند ذلك الطلب، ولم يفعله قبل ذلك الطلب فحصل الدوران بينهما وجودًا وعدما، والدوران وجودًا وعدما يدل على العلية. إلا أنا نقول قد بينا أن الدوران وجودا وعدمًا لا يدل على العلية والذي يحقق ذلك أنه لا يبعد أن يقال : لما التمس ذلك المدعي من ذلك الملك أن يحرك قلنسوته، فإنه حصل في ذلك الوقت سبب آخر، واستقل بأن يحرك الملك قلنسوته لأجله، لا لأجل طلب المدعي، إما لأجل أن عقربا وقع على قلنسوته في ذلك الوقت، أو لأجل انه تألم رأسه من تلك القلنسوة في ذلك الوقت، أو لأجل أن إنسانًا آخر أشار إليه إشارة اقتضت أن يحرك الملك قلنسوته وبالجملة فالمعلوم أنه لا بد للملك من غرض في تحريك القلنسوة، فأما انه لا غرض إلا تصديق هذا المدعي ، فهذا لا شك أنه ظاهر، وأما القطع فلا سبيل إليه. المقام الثاني : هب أنه ثبت هذا المعنى في الشاهد فكيف يقيسون الغائب عليه ؟ وطريق السؤال عليه من وجهين : الأول : أن القياس لا يفيد اليقين لاحتمال أن ما لأجله وقعت المفارقة بين الأصل والفرع معتبر في الأصل أو مانع في الفرع. الثاني : أنا عرفنا بأحوال ذلك الملك وبأخلاقه ومناهج أفعاله، فلا جرم يمكننا أن تعرف أنه إنما فعل ذلك الفعل لأجل ذلك الغرض، وأما أنواع حكم الله تعالى في أفعاله ومخلوقاته، فليس لأحد سبيل إلى معرفتها ولا قدرة لأحد على الاطلاع
Then the deniers said: As for what you relied upon of proofs in explaining that miracles indicate truthfulness, the summary of the discourse is that you claimed that in the observed world, the king's undertaking of an extraordinary act indicates that he is confirming the claimant in his claim. And if that is established in the observed world, then you analogize the unseen to it. So let us examine each of these two positions: As for the first position, we say: We do not concede that the appearance of that act from the king indicates that he confirms the claimant in his claim. We do not deny that it suggests that, but that it indicates it definitively is denied. That is because the utmost in the matter is to say: That act occurred at that request, and he did not do it before that request, so correlation in existence and non-existence occurred between them, and correlation in existence and non-existence indicates causality. However, we say: We have explained that correlation in existence and non-existence does not indicate causality. And what verifies that is that it is not far-fetched to say: When that claimant asked that king to move his cap, another cause occurred at that time, and it was sufficient for the king to move his cap for its sake, not for the claimant's request. Either because a scorpion fell on his cap at that time, or because his head hurt from that cap at that time, or because another person gestured to him with a gesture that necessitated that the king move his cap. In short, it is known that the king must have a purpose in moving the cap. As for there being no purpose except confirming this claimant, this is no doubt apparent, but definitive certainty is not attainable. The second position: Suppose this meaning is established in the observed world, how do they analogize the unseen to it? The path of questioning it has two aspects: First: That analogy does not yield certainty because of the possibility that what caused the difference between the original and the branch may be a condition in the original or an impediment in the branch. Second: That we know the conditions of that king, his morals, and the patterns of his actions, so necessarily we can know that he did that act for that purpose. But as for the types of God's wisdom in His actions and creations, no one has a way to know them nor the ability to have insight into them.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage presents the deniers' response to the argument that miracles prove a claimant's truthfulness by analogical reasoning from observable kingship. The deniers break the argument into two positions: the observed world and the unseen. In the first position, they reject that a king's extraordinary act definitively indicates confirmation of a claimant. They argue that correlation between the request and the act does not prove causation, as concurrent alternate causes (like a scorpion or headache) could explain the act. Thus, while the act may suggest confirmation, it cannot prove it. In the second position, even if the first were granted, the analogy from a known king to an unknown divine actor fails because analogy does not yield certainty due to possible differences. Moreover, we can understand a king's motives from knowing his character, but God's purposes are beyond human comprehension.

Key Terms
الدوران
العلية
الشاهد
الظن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If an extraordinary event happens at the same time as a claim, it must be caused by the claim.
Correction: The deniers show that other causes could coincide, so the conjunction does not prove causation.

Checkpoint
According to the deniers, why does the king's moving his cap at the request not definitively prove he is confirming the claimant?
A) Because the king might have a headache and needed to adjust his cap.
B) Because the king always moves his cap when asked.
C) Because the claimant did not actually make a request.
D) Because the king is not aware of the request.

Key Takeaway
Mere correlation between a miracle and a claim does not prove causality; alternative explanations always remain possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you automatically assumed that a person's action was caused by the most obvious reason (like the claimant's request), without considering alternative explanations? How does this lesson challenge you to be more cautious in your judgments?
?
This passage shows that even a miraculous-looking event does not give certainty about its cause. Is there an area of your life where you have been demanding certainty from ambiguous signs? What would it mean for you to accept probability (zhann) instead?
?
The deniers argue that the king's act could have been caused by a scorpion, headache, or another person's gesture. Can you think of a time you interpreted a blessing or trial from Allah as a direct response to your du'a, when it might have had another cause? How does this lesson affect your relationship with Allah's signs in your life?

Analogy from King to God Fails
Deniers critique the analogy from observed kings to unseen divine acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the deniers' two criticisms of analogical reasoning from observed human kings to the unseen divine realm: the uncertainty of analogy and the hiddenness of God's wisdom.

Core Idea
Analogy from the observed world cannot unlock divine purposes.

Even if one grants that a king's extraordinary act indicates confirmation in the observable world, the deniers argue that this cannot be extended to God. They present two independent objections: first, analogical reasoning (qiyas) does not yield certainty due to potential differences; second, we can understand human kings through their character, but God's ways are completely beyond our knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the second position passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the second position. Notice how the deniers first grant a hypothetical concession ('hubb' – suppose it is established) and then attack the analogy.
2
Examine the first objection: analogy lacks certainty
The deniers state that analogy does not produce certainty because there may be a condition in the original case or an impediment in the new case that invalidates the comparison.
This is a classic critique of analogical reasoning in jurisprudence (usul al-fiqh).
3
Examine the second objection: hiddenness of God's wisdom
We can know a human king's morals and patterns from observation, but no one has insight into God's wisdom or the reasons behind His actions.
This highlights the fundamental difference between human and divine.

Razi's Refutation of the Impossibility of Miracles
Razi presents an objection against miracles and refutes it with two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the objection that miracles (breaking habits) are impossible by appealing to the possibility of events on celestial bodies and the principle of homogeneity of physical bodies.

Core Idea
Miracles are not impossible because what is possible for one body is possible for all.

In this passage, Imam Razi addresses a common objection against miracles: that they constitute an impossible 'breaking of habits.' He provides a concise two-part answer, referencing the possibility of such events on celestial bodies and the principle of homogeneity among bodies. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the objection and the two-part answer.
2
Examine the first proof
Notice that Razi first reminds the reader that celestial bodies can undergo 'kharq wa ilti'am' (splitting and healing), citing a prior demonstration.
This refers to events like the splitting of the moon.
3
Examine the second proof
Then he invokes the universal principle: any event possible for one body is possible for all bodies of the same type.
This extends the possibility from celestial to terrestrial bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله أولا : " انخراق العادات "محال قلنا : قد بينا أن الخرق والالتئام على الأجرام الفلكية جائزان، وأيضًا بينا أن ما يصح على جسم يصح على سائر الأجسام.
He said firstly: "Breaking of habits is impossible." We said: We have already shown that breaking and healing are possible for celestial bodies, and also we have shown that what is possible for one body is possible for all bodies.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its response. The objector claims that miracles, as 'breaking of habits' (khāriq al-ʿādāt), are impossible. The answer refutes this by recalling an earlier proof that celestial bodies can undergo breaking and healing (e.g., the splitting of the moon). Furthermore, the principle of homogeneity is invoked: any event possible for one body is possible for all bodies of the same nature. Thus miracles are not categorically impossible.

Key Terms
انخراق العادات
الأجرام الفلكية
الخرق والالتئام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles are impossible because they break the natural habits that are necessary.
Correction: Razi shows that habits are not necessary; they are contingent and can be broken by divine power, as evidenced by events on celestial bodies and the principle of homogeneity.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for Razi's response to the objection that breaking habits is impossible?
A) Miracles are purely conceptual and not physical.
B) Celestial bodies are subject to breaking and healing, and all bodies share the same possibilities.
C) The objector's argument is irrelevant because habits are not real.
D) Miracles only appear to break habits but actually follow hidden natural laws.

Key Takeaway
Miracles are not categorically impossible; they are possible because the same types of events can occur in celestial bodies and by extension in all bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You say you believe God can do anything, but when you face an impossible situation in your own life—a broken relationship, a stubborn sin, a hopeless career—do you genuinely expect a miracle, or do you settle for 'this is just how things are'? What would it mean for you to truly believe that what is possible for one body is possible for all bodies, including yours?
?
If miracles are possible because celestial bodies can break and heal, why do you doubt that God can break the habits of your own heart? What is one specific area of your life where you have unconsciously declared a miracle impossible? Will you today ask God to break that habit, trusting that He can do what you thought was unchangeable?
?
Razi argues that the breaking of habits is not impossible because it already occurs in celestial bodies. Are you willing to see the small 'miracles' around you as evidence that God can do the extraordinary in your life? When was the last time you thanked God for a 'break of habit' that you took for granted?

Objection and Answer: Miracles as Direct Proofs
Razi records an objection and answers it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that if God alone creates miracles, the eight reasons become invalid, and Razi's response that the miracle itself is the direct proof.

Core Idea
The miracle itself suffices; the eight reasons are irrelevant.

In this passage, Imam Razi continues his defense of miracles as proofs of prophethood. An objector argues that if God is the sole agent of miracles, then the eight reasons previously given for verifying a prophet become pointless. Razi's crisp answer reveals a key Ashʿarite principle: the miracle itself is the divine endorsement, needing no additional reasoning. Let's examine how he turns the objection into a reinforcement of his position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation carefully. Notice the concise structure: a question from the objector followed by a direct answer.
This is a typical kalam objection-and-answer format.
2
Identify the objection
The objector claims that if God is the creator of miracles, then the eight reasons (al-wujūh al-thamāniyya) used to prove prophethood become invalid. Understand why this seems problematic: if God does everything, how can the miracle point to the prophet?
The eight reasons were previously listed; here they are referenced but not repeated.
3
Analyze the answer
Razi states that the divine principle 'no agent but God' in fact makes the miracle a direct proof. The eight reasons are not needed because the miracle itself, as an act of God, immediately confirms the prophet's truthfulness. This is a key demonstration of Ashʿarite epistemology.
Notice the word 'tasquṭ'—they 'fall away' not because they are false, but because they become unnecessary.
4
Reflect on the broader argument
Consider how this response fits into Razi's overall defense of miracles. The objection is turned into an affirmation that miracles are self-sufficient proofs, requiring no additional argumentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله ثانيا : " ثم قلتم : إن موجد هذه المعجزات هو الله تعالى ؟ قلنا : قد بينا فيما تقدم أنه لا مؤثر إلا قدرة الله وعلى هذا التقدير تسقط الوجوه الثمانية التي ذكرتموها.
He said secondly: "Then you said that the Creator of these miracles is Allah." We said: We have shown previously that there is no agent except the power of Allah, and on this assumption, the eight aspects you mentioned fall away.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection and its answer. The objector notes that the proponent of miracles (likely the Ashʿarite) attributes miracles solely to God. If so, the objector argues, the eight reasons (al-wujūh al-thamāniyya) they had listed for proving prophethood become invalid. Razi responds by reaffirming the Ashʿarite principle that no effect (lā muʾaththir) exists except God's power. Therefore, the eight reasons are not needed; the argument from miracles is direct and does not depend on those reasons. Thus the objection misses the mark.

Key Terms
مؤثر
قدرة الله
الوجوه الثمانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if God alone creates miracles, then no connection exists between the miracle and the claimant's truthfulness.
Correction: Razi's answer shows that the miracle itself is the direct proof; the eight reasons are unnecessary because the miracle is an act of God that endorses the prophet.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi say the eight reasons fall away?
A) Because they were never valid.
B) Because on the assumption that God alone is the agent, the miracle directly proves truthfulness, making the eight reasons unnecessary.
C) Because the objector has misunderstood the eight reasons.
D) Because the eight reasons only apply to natural events.

Key Takeaway
The miracle itself is a direct proof of prophethood; no additional reasons are needed when God is the sole agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a clear sign from God—like a miracle or an answered prayer—do you still seek additional human proofs before you are convinced? What does that say about your trust in His power? Will you let the miracle itself be enough, or will you demand the eight reasons? This week, when you see something clearly from God, commit to accepting it directly without overthinking.
?
The objector thought that if God is the sole agent, then the eight reasons become invalid. But Razi said they are simply irrelevant. Have you ever dismissed a truth from God because it didn't fit your own intellectual framework? Are you willing to let go of your own 'eight reasons' and submit to the direct evidence of His power?
?
The passage says 'no agent except God's power.' Do you live as if this is true? Or do you attribute causes to people, systems, and yourself? How would your life change if you truly believed that every outcome comes directly from Allah? Challenge yourself to act on that belief today.

Objection and Answer on God's Acts and Purposes
Razi records the objection that God's acts lack purposes, and answers with the analogy of speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the objection that God's acts lack purposes by distinguishing between purpose and indication.

Core Idea
Miracles indicate truthfulness; they are not necessarily created for a purpose.

This lesson presents a key objection to the use of miracles as proof of prophethood: if God's acts are not motivated by purposes, then miracles cannot serve as evidence. Imam Razi offers a nuanced response that separates the concept of purpose from the concept of indication. By drawing an analogy with language, he shows that miracles can indicate the truthfulness of a prophet without implying that God created them for that specific purpose.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Start by reading the objector's claim: that God's acts are not motivated by purposes. This challenges the prophetic use of miracles.
The objection is third in a series.
2
Identify Razi's key distinction
Notice how Razi distinguishes between a 'cause' (ʿilla) and an 'indication'. He does not claim the miracle is created for verification, but that it makes verification known.
This distinction is crucial to answering the objection.
3
Understand the analogy with speech
Razi compares miraculous acts to words: just as words indicate meanings by convention, miracles indicate truthfulness by occurring after the prophetic claim.
The analogy highlights the indicative function without assuming purpose.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this answer preserves the evidential value of miracles while avoiding the problem of attributing purposes to God. How does it strengthen the proof of prophethood?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله ثالثًا : " أن أفعال الله غير معللة بالأغراض" قلنا : فرق بين العلة المعروف ونحن لا ندعي أن خلق المعجز إنما كان لغرض التصديق، بل نقول: خلق المعجزة يعرف قيام التصديق بذات الله تعالى وكما أن هذه الكلمات المخصوصة صارت دالة بحسب الوضع والاصطلاح على المعانى القائمة بذات المتكلم، فكذلك هذه الأفعال الخارقة للعادات إذا حصلت عقيب الدعوى صارت دالة على قيام التصديق بذات من فعل المعجز.
He said thirdly: "The acts of Allah are not motivated by purposes." We said: There is a difference between the known cause (al-ʿilla al-maʿrūf) and we do not claim that the creation of the miracle was only for the purpose of verification; rather, we say: The creation of the miracle makes known the existence of verification in the essence of Allah, just as these specific words become indicative, by convention and custom, of the meanings subsisting in the essence of the speaker, so likewise these extraordinary acts, when they occur after the claim, become indicative of the existence of verification in the essence of the one who performs the miracle.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage presents a third objection and its answer. The objector argues that God's actions are not motivated by purposes (aghraḍ), so miracles cannot be intended to confirm a prophet. Razi distinguishes between a true cause (ʿilla) and a mere indicator. The Ashʿarite position is not that the miracle is created for the purpose of verifying the prophet; rather, the miracle itself, by its occurrence after the claim, indicates (yadullu) that the claimant is truthful, just as words (alfāẓ) indicate the speaker's meaning by convention. Thus, the relationship is not one of purpose but of natural or conventional indication. This avoids the objection that God's acts are purposeless because the indication is not a purpose but an inherent consequence.

Key Terms
العلة
الأغراض
الوضع والاصطلاح
الخارقة للعادات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that if God's acts have no purposes, then miracles cannot be evidence for prophethood.
Correction: Razi argues that miracles are not created for the purpose of verification; rather, they indicate verification by convention, just as words indicate meanings.

Checkpoint
How does Razi answer the objection that God's acts have no purposes?
A) He admits that miracles are purposeless but still prove prophethood.
B) He distinguishes between purpose and indication, stating miracles indicate truthfulness like words indicate meanings.
C) He argues that God does have purposes in miracles.
D) He says the objection is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
Razi's distinction between purpose and indication preserves the evidential value of miracles without attributing purposes to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face an unexpected trial or blessing, do you immediately ask 'Why did God do this to me?' instead of seeing it as a sign pointing to His wisdom and mercy? How would your heart change if you stopped demanding purposes and instead looked for indications?
?
This lesson shows that miracles are not created to convince you—they simply indicate truth by their occurrence. Does your faith depend on seeing clear purposes behind events, or can you trust the signs God gives, even when you don't understand the reason?
?
Razi's analogy with speech means words indicate meaning by convention, not by divine cause. When you read the Qur'an or hear a sermon, do you treat it as a purposeful divine act or as a sign pointing to the Speaker's attributes? Which posture brings you closer to Him?

Answering the Objection That Miracles Have Other Purposes
Razi records the objection and answers with an example from Moses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi defends the exclusive purpose of miracles in verifying prophethood using a concrete example from Moses.

Core Idea
A miracle's purpose is unmistakably to verify the prophet, not anything else.

In this passage, Razi addresses a fourth objection: perhaps miracles are created for purposes other than verifying the prophet's truthfulness. He firmly rejects this, arguing that the connection is so clear it becomes necessary knowledge. He illustrates with a striking hypothetical example involving Prophet Moses, where the miracle's behavior directly correlates with people's belief.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Razi's response
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Razi first states the objection, then immediately rejects it with 'No, we do not accept'.
The objection is the fourth in a series.
2
Understand 'mutaʿayyin' (specifically for indication)
Razi asserts that the miracle is 'mutaʿayyin' – specifically designated – for indicating verification. This means its purpose is not merely possible but necessary and exclusive.
This is a strong claim: miracles cannot have other purposes.
3
Analyze the Moses example
Examine the hypothetical scenario: Moses asks God to suspend a mountain over the people, and the mountain's position changes with their belief or disbelief. This visible correlation makes the purpose immediately known to everyone.
Razi argues this leads to 'necessary knowledge' (ʿilm ḍarūrī).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله رابعا : " يحتمل أن يكون المقصود من خلق المعجزات أمورًا اخر سوى التصديق قلنا : لا ،نسلم بل هو متعين للدلالة على حصول التصديق، والدليل عليه أن موسى الا لما قال : " إلهي إن كنت صادقا في ادعاء الرسالة فاجعل هذا الجبل في الهواء فوق رؤوسهم ثم إن القوم يشاهدون أنهم كلما آمنوا به تباعد الجبل عنهم، وكلما هموا بتكذيبه قرب أن يسقط عليهم، فعند هذا يعلم كل أحد بالضرورة [أن] المقصود من [هذا] الأطلال[؟] تصديق المدعي في ادعاء الرسالة.
He said fourthly: "It is possible that the purpose of creating miracles is other than verification." We said: No, we do not accept; rather it is specifically for indicating the occurrence of verification. The evidence for it is that when Moses said: "My Lord, if I am truthful in claiming prophethood, then place this mountain in the air above their heads," then the people would observe that whenever they believed in him, the mountain moved away from them, and whenever they intended to deny him, it nearly fell upon them. Therefore, everyone knows necessarily that the purpose of that [miracle] is verification of the claimant in his claim of prophethood.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage presents a fourth objection: miracles might have other purposes besides verifying the prophet. Razi responds by rejecting this possibility and asserting that the miracle is specifically (mutaʿayyin) for indicating verification. He provides a concrete example: the hypothetical miracle of Moses suspending a mountain over the people, where the mountain's behavior varies with their belief or disbelief. This causes everyone to know necessarily (bi-ḍarūra) that the miracle's purpose is to verify the claimant. The example illustrates that the connection between the miracle and truthfulness is so clear that it becomes a matter of immediate knowledge.

Key Terms
متعين
الدلالة
علم ضروري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles could be ambiguous or have multiple possible purposes, such as testing people or displaying power.
Correction: Razi argues that miracles are specifically (mutaʿayyin) for indicating the truthfulness of the prophet, as shown by the clear correlation in the Moses example, leading to necessary knowledge.

Checkpoint
What evidence does Razi give for the miracle being specifically for indicating truthfulness?
A) A logical syllogism.
B) A hypothetical example of Moses making a mountain respond to people's belief.
C) A quote from the Quran.
D) A consensus of scholars.

Key Takeaway
Miracles are necessarily and exclusively for verifying the prophet's truthfulness, as demonstrated by the clear correlation in the Moses example.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a clear sign from Allah because you wanted a different kind of miracle? How does Razi's insistence that miracles have one unmistakable purpose challenge your own expectations of how Allah should guide you?
?
If the mountain example shows that your belief or disbelief directly affects the signs you see, what does that say about your own perception of miracles today? Are you actively looking for signs of truth, or are you indifferent?
?
The passage says everyone 'knows necessarily' that the miracle verifies the prophet. But do you truly accept that knowledge in your heart, or do you still rationalize away divine signs when they demand a response from you?
