The Fifth Objection: God Creating Disbelief Invalidates Miracles
Razi records the fifth objection concisely.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth objection against the Ashʿarite view that God creates disbelief, and why it threatens the evidentiary value of miracles.

Core Idea
If God creates disbelief, miracles cannot prove truthfulness.

The objector argues that if disbelief and sin are created by God, then a miracle could be shown at the hand of a liar, making it impossible to infer truthfulness from miracles. This lesson presents the objection itself, which challenges the very foundation of prophetic proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic and translation of the objection (the first sentence of the passage). Note the concise phrasing.
The objection is a conditional: if disbelief is created by God, then miracle-based inference fails.
2
Identify the logical threat
Reflect on why this objection is serious for Ashʿarites: if God can create a miracle at a liar's hand, how can any miracle be a reliable sign of truth?
3
Consider the broader debate
The objector likely assumes that miracles must have the purpose of verification. Ask yourself: Could a miracle have other purposes? This prepares you for Razi's answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله خامسا : " إذا كان الكفر والفسق بخلق الله تعالى فحينئذ بطل الاستدلال بالمعجز على التصديق " قلنا : الجواب عنه من وجهين : الأول: أن هذا السؤال كما أنه لازم علينا فهو أيضًا لازم على المعتزلة، وذلك لأنه إنما يقبح من الله إظهار هذه المعجزة على يد الكاذب، إذا كان الغرض من خلق المعجزة تصديق المدعي، وأما بتقدير أن يكون الغرض منه شيئًا آخر سوى التصديق لم يقبح ذلك، ولما ثبت أنه لا يبعد ان يكون لله تعالى أغراض أخر من هذا المعجز سوى التصديق يلزم أن يحكموا بأنه لا يقبح إظهاره على يد الكاذب. لا يقال : إظهار المعجزة على وفق دعوى الكذب قبيح مطلقا ؛ لأنه إن كان الغرض من خلقه هو التصديق فلا شك في قبحه، وإن كان الغرض منه شيئًا آخر سوى التصديق لكنه خلقه مقارنا لدعوى الكاذب يوهم أن الغرض منه التصديق ففعل ما يوهم تصديق الكاذب قبيح أيضًا. لأنا نقول : إن كان ما يوهم القبيح قبيحا وجب أن يكون إنزال المتشابهات وخلق ما يوهم الشبهات قبيحًا، وبالاتفاق أنه واقع وليس بقبيح، فعلمنا أنه إنما لم لأنه وإن كان موهما للباطل إلا أن فيه احتمال أن مراد الله تعالى منه غير مات يشعر به ظاهره فلو قطع المكلف بحكمه على ذلك توجه الباطل، مع أنه في نفسه محتمل لغير ذلك الوجه لكان التقصير من المكلف حيث جزم لا في موضع الجزم، وكذا ههنا أنه تعالى إذا أظهر المعجزة على وفق الدعوى في حق الكاذب فهو وإن كان المراد منه تصديق ذلك المدعي إلا أن يكون المراد القاء هذه الشبهة حتى يحترز المكلف منها، فيعظم ثوابه بسبب الاحتراز عن هذه الشبهة، وإذا كان هذا مستحيلا فلم قطع المكلف بأن الغرض منه تصديق المدعي لكان التقصير منه لا الله تعالى، حيث قطع لا في موضع القطع. واعلم أنه لا جواب على أصول المعتزلة عن هذه المعارضة.
He said fifthly: "If disbelief and sin are created by Allah, then the inference from a miracle to truthfulness becomes invalid." We said: The answer has two aspects. The first: This objection, as it is necessary for us, is also necessary for the Mu'tazila. That is because it is only reprehensible for Allah to produce this miracle at the hand of a liar if the purpose of creating the miracle was to verify the claimant. But assuming that the purpose is something other than verification, it is not reprehensible. And since it is established that it is not far-fetched for Allah to have other purposes from this miracle besides verification, they [the Mu'tazila] must judge that it is not reprehensible to produce it at the hand of a liar. It cannot be said: Producing a miracle in accordance with a liar's claim is absolutely reprehensible; because if the purpose of its creation is verification, then there is no doubt about its evil; and if the purpose is something else besides verification, yet He created it concurrent with the liar's claim so that it suggests that the purpose is verification, then doing what suggests verifying a liar is also evil. For we say: If what suggests evil is itself evil, then it would be necessary that sending down ambiguous verses (mutashābihāt) and creating things that suggest misconceptions would be evil, but by agreement they occur and are not evil. So we know that it is only because, even though it suggests falsehood, there is a possibility that Allah's intention from it is other than what its apparent meaning suggests. So if the responsible person (mukallaf) makes a definite judgment about it, falsehood would follow, while in itself it is possible for that other aspect. Then the shortcoming would be from the responsible person because he made a definite judgment where no definite judgment is warranted. Similarly here, when Allah produces a miracle in accordance with the claim in the case of a liar, even if the intended meaning is to verify that claimant, it could also be that the intended meaning is to cast this doubt so that the responsible person guards against it, and thus his reward becomes great due to his guarding against this doubt. And if this is not improbable, then if the responsible person decides that the purpose is to verify the claimant, the shortcoming would be from him, not from Allah, because he made a definite judgment where no definite judgment is warranted. Know that there is no answer according to the principles of the Mu'tazila against this objection.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage presents a fifth objection and the first of two answers. The objector claims that if disbelief and sin are created by God (the Ashʿarite view), then miracles cannot prove truthfulness because God could create a miracle at the hand of a liar. Razi's first answer has two parts: First, the same objection applies to the Mu'tazila, because if God's purpose in a miracle is not necessarily to verify the claimant, then showing it at a liar's hand is not evil. Second, even if the miracle suggests verification, it is not necessarily evil because it might have another purpose, such as testing the responsible person. Razi uses the analogy of ambiguous Quranic verses (mutashābihāt), which are allowed despite suggesting falsehood because they serve a greater purpose. The fault lies with the person who makes a definite judgment without certainty. Thus, the miracle can still indicate truthfulness for those who are cautious. He concludes that the Mu'tazila have no effective response to this.

Key Terms
الكفر والفسق
المعجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God creates everything, then miracles are meaningless because God could support a liar.
Correction: The objection assumes that God has no purpose other than verification when creating a miracle. Razi will later argue that God may have other purposes, and the burden of correct judgment lies on the observer.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence stated in the objection if disbelief is created by God?
A) Miracles become even stronger proofs.
B) Inference from miracles to truthfulness becomes invalid.
C) Prophets can no longer perform miracles.
D) God is unjust.

Key Takeaway
The fifth objection claims that if God creates disbelief, miracles cannot serve as proofs of truthfulness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God creates all disbelief, then your own unbelief (if any) is from Him. How does that truth change your view of those who reject Islam? Do you feel more compassion or more frustration? What does this say about your reliance on God for guidance?
?
The objection says miracles become useless if God creates disbelief. Have you ever relied on a miracle (like the Quran's inimitability) to strengthen your faith? What would it mean for your faith if that foundation were removed?
?
When you sin, do you blame God's creation or your own choice? This objection forces you to decide. Which answer makes you more accountable to change? Pray about that tonight.

Razi's First Answer: Miracles Can Have Multiple Purposes
Razi provides the first of two answers, using an analogy with ambiguous verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's first answer to the fifth objection, including his use of the analogy of ambiguous Quranic verses and the shifting of responsibility to the observer.

Core Idea
Miracles remain valid because the fault lies with the observer who judges without certainty.

Razi responds to the objection by first noting that it applies equally to the Mu'tazila, then argues that a miracle can have purposes beyond verification, just like ambiguous Quranic verses (mutashābihāt). If a person mistakenly concludes that the miracle verifies a liar, the shortcoming is his own, not God's.

Guided Path
1
Read the full answer passage
Read the Arabic and translation of the response from 'قلنا: الجواب عنه من وجهين' to the end.
2
Understand the two sub-arguments
The answer has two parts: (1) The objection also applies to Mu'tazila because they must allow other purposes for miracles, (2) A miracle can have a purpose other than verification, such as testing believers.
The second part is explained with the analogy of mutashābihāt.
3
Analyze the analogy of ambiguous verses
Razi compares the situation to ambiguous Quranic verses that might suggest falsehood but are not evil because they serve wisdom. The fault is on the one who misinterprets.
4
Reflect on responsibility
The key conclusion: if a miracle appears to support a liar, the observer who judges definitely without certainty is at fault. The miracle still indicates truth to the cautious.

Source Text

He said fifthly: "If disbelief and sin are created by Allah, then the inference from a miracle to truthfulness becomes invalid." We said: The answer has two aspects. The first: This objection, as it is necessary for us, is also necessary for the Mu'tazila. That is because it is only reprehensible for Allah to produce this miracle at the hand of a liar if the purpose of creating the miracle was to verify the claimant. But assuming that the purpose is something other than verification, it is not reprehensible. Then the shortcoming would be from the responsible person because he made a definite judgment where no definite judgment is warranted. Know that there is no answer according to the principles of the Mu'tazila against this objection.

Commentary
This passage presents a fifth objection and the first of two answers. The objector claims that if disbelief and sin are created by God (the Ashʿarite view), then miracles cannot prove truthfulness because God could create a miracle at the hand of a liar. Razi's first answer has two parts: First, the same objection applies to the Mu'tazila, because if God's purpose in a miracle is not necessarily to verify the claimant, then showing it at a liar's hand is not evil. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any action that suggests falsehood is intrinsically evil.
Correction: Razi shows that ambiguous verses suggest falsehood but are not evil because they have possible alternate meanings and serve a higher purpose. The fault lies with the observer's hasty judgment.

What analogy does Razi use to argue that suggesting falsehood is not inherently evil?
A) A father's punishment.
B) The creation of celestial bodies.
C) Ambiguous Quranic verses (mutashābihāt).
D) A liar's own statements.

Key Takeaway
Miracles can still indicate truth because if an observer mistakenly takes a miracle as verifying a liar, the blame is on his own lack of certainty, not on God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you made a definite judgment about God's purpose in a situation without having certainty? How did that affect your attitude toward Him?
?
This lesson shows that ambiguous situations can test our faith. What is one doubt or confusion you are currently facing about your religion? Are you willing to suspend judgment and seek deeper understanding, or do you rush to a conclusion?
?
If Allah placed a sign that could be misinterpreted, and He holds you accountable for that misinterpretation, are you actively guarding your heart against hasty conclusions? What practical step can you take today to avoid making definite judgments where none are warranted?

The Premise: Logical Possibility vs. Necessary Knowledge
Razi presents a premise about necessary knowledge with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's premise that something can be logically possible yet known by necessary knowledge not to occur, and how this distinction underpins his defense of miracles.

Core Idea
Logical possibility does not undermine necessary knowledge.

Before answering the objection directly, Razi lays a conceptual foundation. He argues that we can know with certainty that certain events occur, even though their opposites are logically possible. The example of an elderly person's existence illustrates this: while it is possible in theory for a person to appear without parents, we know necessarily that every elderly person we see was born and grew through stages. This premise is crucial for the argument about miracles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise
Read the opening lines where Razi states the premise: a thing may be possible to occur yet known by necessary knowledge not to occur. Notice the shift from logical possibility to actual knowledge.
The Arabic uses 'جائز الوقوع' for possible and 'علم ضروري' for necessary knowledge.
2
Examine the example
Consider the example of an elderly person. Razi says we can imagine someone coming into existence without parents or aging, but when we see an actual elderly person, we know necessarily that he was born and passed through life stages. Reflect on why this knowledge is 'necessary' rather than inferred.
This shows that necessary knowledge is based on direct observation of customary patterns.
3
Generalize the principle
Razi states that this applies to all customary matters (الأمور العادية). Think about other examples: e.g., we know the sun will rise tomorrow even though it's logically possible it won't. The principle is that necessary knowledge overrides bare logical possibilities.
This principle is the foundation for the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني في الجواب عن هذا السؤال : من انا نقدم على ذكر المقصود ،مقدمة، فنقول : إن الشيء قد يكون جائز الوقوع في ذلك فإنا نعلم علما ضروريًا أنه غير واقع ألا ترى أنا نجوز دخول ومع شخص في الوجود من غير أبوين، ونجوز أن يدخل في الوجود شيخ هرم من غير سبق الطفولية والشباب والكهولة، ثم إنا إذا أبصرنا شيخا علمنا بالضرورة أنه متولد من الأبوين، وأنه كان طفلا، ثم صار شاباً ثم صار شيخاً، وكذا القول في جميع الأمور العادية. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إنا قد بينا أن دلالة المعجزة على أن خلق المعجز لصدق المدعي معلوم بالضرورة كما ضربنا من المثال في إظلال الجبال، أقصى ما في الباب أن يقال عنه : يجوز أن تكون المعجزة مخلوقة لغرض آخر وهو الإضلال إلا أنا نقول : إن الشيء إذا علم وجوده بالضرورة لم يكن تجويز نقيضه يقدح في ذلك العلم الضروري كما بيناه في هذه المقدمة.
The second aspect of the answer to this question: We present a premise before stating the intended point. We say: A thing may be possible to occur, yet we know with necessary knowledge that it does not occur. Do you not see that we allow the entry of a person into existence without parents, and we allow the entry into existence of an elderly old man without prior childhood, youth, and middle age? Yet when we see an elderly man, we know necessarily that he is born from parents, and that he was a child, then became a youth, then became an old man. And the same applies to all customary matters. When you understand this, we say: We have already shown that the indication of the miracle that the creation of the miracle is for the truthfulness of the claimant is known necessarily, as we gave the example of the mountain's shadow. The most that can be said about it is: It is possible that the miracle is created for another purpose, namely misguidance. However, we say: When a thing is known by necessary knowledge, the possibility of its opposite does not undermine that necessary knowledge, as we explained in this premise.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage is the second answer to the fifth objection. Razi first establishes a premise: some events are possible in themselves (jāʾiz al-wuqūʿ) but are known by necessary knowledge (ʿilm ḍarūrī) not to actually occur. For example, it is possible for a person to come into existence without parents, yet we know necessarily that every elderly person we see was born from parents and passed through stages of life. Similarly, despite the theoretical possibility that a miracle could serve a different purpose (e.g., misguidance), we know necessarily that the miracle indicates the truthfulness of the claimant, as illustrated by the earlier example of the mountain. The mere logical possibility of an alternative does not overturn this necessary knowledge. Thus, the objection fails because it only presents a bare possibility, not a real doubt.

Key Terms
علم ضروري
جائز الوقوع
الأمور العادية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if something is logically possible, then we cannot be certain it is false.
Correction: Razi shows that necessary knowledge can coexist with logical possibility; we can know with certainty that something does not occur even if its opposite is conceivable.

Checkpoint
What example does Razi use to illustrate that something can be logically possible yet known necessarily not to occur?
A) A mountain moving.
B) An elderly person being born from parents.
C) A miracle being created by God.
D) The breaking of habits.

Key Takeaway
Necessary knowledge of actual events is not undermined by the logical possibility of their opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold as certain in your daily life—like that the sun will rise tomorrow. Have you ever let a 'what if' hypothetical (e.g., 'but maybe it won't') subtly erode your certainty? Where else in your faith are you letting logical possibilities weaken your necessary knowledge? What step will you take today to reaffirm that certainty?
?
When you witness the regularity of creation—a child growing, seasons changing—do you recognize it as a sign of Allah's custom and respond with gratitude, or do you take it for granted? How can you turn that necessary knowledge into active worship starting this moment?
?
The passage shows that even though miracles are logically possible, our necessary knowledge of customs remains intact. Is there a miracle from the Qur'an or Sunnah that you struggle to accept because 'it just doesn't happen'? How will you train yourself to trust Allah's power over your limited experience of what is 'customary'?

Applying the Premise: Miracles and Necessary Knowledge
Razi applies the premise about necessary knowledge to the miracle objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi applies his premise about necessary knowledge to defend the evidentiary value of miracles against the objection that they could be created for misguidance.

Core Idea
Necessary knowledge of a miracle's purpose is immune to bare possibilities.

Having established the premise that necessary knowledge overrides logical possibilities, Razi now applies it directly to the miracle objection. He reminds us that we have already shown that the indication of a miracle for the truthfulness of the claimant is known necessarily — like knowing a mountain's shadow indicates its presence. The objector says a miracle could be created for misguidance, but Razi counters that this mere possibility does not undermine the necessary knowledge we have. This lesson completes the second answer.

Guided Path
1
Recall the premise
Before reading the application, quickly review the premise from the previous lesson: something can be logically possible yet known by necessary knowledge not to occur.
This connection is essential to understand the argument.
2
Read the application
Read from 'إذا عرفت هذا' to the end. Razi says we have already shown that the miracle's indication is known necessarily. The objector's only point is that it is possible the miracle is for misguidance. But necessary knowledge is not weakened by mere possibility.
Notice the structure: 'أقصى ما في الباب أن يقال عنه' introduces the objection's strongest point.
3
Understand the conclusion
Razi concludes that the objection fails because it only offers a logical possibility, not a real doubt. The necessary knowledge we have about miracles remains intact. Reflect on how this argument protects the use of miracles as proof of prophethood.
This is a powerful defense because it shifts the burden: the objector must do more than suggest a possibility.

The First Proof for Karamat al-Awliya: The Case of Maryam
Razi states disagreement on karamat and gives the first proof from Maryam with four refutations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses the story of Maryam (peace be upon her) as evidence for karamat al-awliya and how he defends it against the objection that these events could be prophetic miracles for Zakariyya (peace be upon him).

Core Idea
Maryam's miracles prove saints can perform miracles because she was not a prophet.

Imam Razi begins his defense of karamat al-awliya (miracles of saints) by presenting the first of three proofs. He focuses on the extraordinary events in the life of Maryam—her pregnancy without a male and her receiving sustenance miraculously. Since Maryam was not a prophet, these events must be classified as karamat. He then anticipates and refutes the objection that they could be mu'jizat (prophetic miracles) for Zakariyya. This lesson walks through Razi's argument and his four counter-points.

Guided Path
1
Understand the disagreement
Read the opening lines to learn the positions of the Mu'tazila and Ash'aris on karamat. Note that most Ash'aris affirm them, but al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq disagreed.
This sets the stage for why proof is needed.
2
Read the first proof
Identify how Razi uses Maryam's story: her pregnancy without a male and provision from unseen are khariq al-'adah, and since she was not a prophet, they must be karamat.
The proof hinges on her non-prophethood.
3
Consider the objection
Notice the hypothetical objection: these events could be mu'jizat for Zakariyya. Why might someone argue this?
The objection challenges the conclusion.
4
Analyze the four refutations
Examine each of Razi's four reasons why the objection fails: (1) miracles must be public, (2) prophet must be present, (3) miracle must be requested, (4) Quranic context honors Maryam.
Each refutation is based on the nature of miracles and Quranic evidence.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how this proof supports the overall doctrine of karamat and why the distinction between mu'jizah and karamah matters.
This connects to the broader theological framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الرابعة والثلاثون في كرامات الأولياء المعتزلة ينكرونها، ووافقهم الأستاذ أبو إسحاق من أصحابنا، وأكثر أصحابنا يثبتونها، وبه قال أبو الحسين البصري من المعتزلة. لنا وجوه ثلاثة : الحجة الأولى : أن حدوث الحبل لمريم من غير الذكر من خوارق العادات، وحضور الرزق عندها من غير سبب ظاهر ومن خوارق العادات وأنها ما كانت من الأنبياء، فوجب أن يقال : إن هذه الوقائع من كرامات الأولياء. فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن تلك الخوارق كانت معجزات لنبي ذلك الزمان، وهو زكريا عليه السلام؟ قلنا : هذا باطل لوجوه. الأول : أن المعجزة لا بد أن تكون أمرًا ظاهرًا للمنكرين حتى يمكن الاستدلال بها على المنكر، وظهور جبريل عليه السلام لمريم وحبلها من غير ذكر، ما كان يطلع عليه أحد إلا مريم فكيف يمكن جعل هذه الأشياء معجزة لزكريا عليه السلام؟ ثانيها : أن عند ظهور المعجز لبعض الأنبياء لا بد أن يكون الرسول حاضرًا، ولا بد أن يكون القوم حاضرين حتى يتمكن ذلك الرسول من الاستدلال بذلك المعجز، وفي الوقت الذي كان يقول جبريل لمريم : ﴿وَهُزَى إِلَيْكِ يجذع النَّخْلَةِ تسقط عليك رطباً جنتا﴾ من كان زكريا حاضرًا هناك حتى يستدل بظهور هذه الخوارق على نبوة نفسه بل ما كان أحد من البشر هناك حاضراً، بدليل قوله تعالى: ﴿فَإِمَّا تَرَينَ مِنَ الْبَشَرِ أَحَدًا فَقُولِي إِنِّي نَذَرْتُ لِلرَّحْمَنِ صَوْمًا﴾ فبطل القول بأن هذه الأشياء معجزة لزكريا عليه السلام. ثالثها: أن حصول المعجز لا بد أن يكون بالتماس الرسول، وكان زكريا عليه السلام غافلا عن كيفية حدوث هذه الأشياء، بدليل قوله تعالى : ﴿كُلَّمَا دَخَلَ عَلَيْهَا زَكَرِيَّا الْمِحْرَابَ وَجَدَ عِندَهَا رِزْقًا قَالَ يَا مَرْيَمُ أَنَّى لَكِ هَذَا قَالَتْ هُوَ مِنْ عِندِ الله﴾ فدل هذا على أن ذلك ما كان معجزا لزكريا عليه السلام. رابعها : أنه تعالى ذكر هذه الخوارق في معرض تعظيم حال مريم ولم يذكر معها ما شعر بجعلها معجزة لأحد من الأنبياء، ولو كان المقصود منها إظهار تصديق زكريا لا إظهار كرامة مريم لكان ذكر زكريا عند ذكر هذه الخوارق أولى من ذكر مريم . ولما لم يكن الأمر كذلك علمنا أن المقصود منها إكرام مريم لا تصديق زكريا.
The thirty-fourth issue: On the miracles of saints. The Mu'tazila deny them, and al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq from our companions agreed with them, while most of our companions affirm them, and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri from the Mu'tazila said likewise. We have three proofs: The first proof: that the pregnancy of Maryam without a male is a breaking of customs, and the arrival of sustenance to her without an apparent cause is a breaking of customs, and she was not from among the prophets, so it is necessary to say: these incidents are from the miracles of saints. If it is said: Why is it not permissible to say that those breaking-of-customs events were miracles for a prophet of that time, namely Zakariyya (peace be upon him)? We say: This is false for reasons. First: a miracle must be a manifest matter to the deniers so that it can be used as evidence against the denier, and the appearance of Jibril to Maryam and her pregnancy without a male, no one was informed of it except Maryam, so how can these things be made a miracle for Zakariyya (peace be upon him)? Second: when a miracle appears for some prophet, the messenger must be present, and the people must be present so that the messenger can use that miracle as evidence, and at the time when Jibril was saying to Maryam: 'And shake towards you the trunk of the palm tree, it will drop upon you ripe dates' [Maryam 25], who was Zakariyya present there so that he could use the appearance of these breaking-of-customs events as evidence for his own prophethood? Rather, no human being was present there, as evidence of His saying: 'And if you see any human being, say: I have vowed to the Most Merciful a fast' [Maryam 26]. So the statement that these things are a miracle for Zakariyya (peace be upon him) is invalidated. Third: the occurrence of a miracle must be upon the request of the messenger, and Zakariyya (peace be upon him) was unaware of how these things happened, as evidence of His saying: 'Whenever Zakariyya entered upon her in the sanctuary, he found with her provision. He said: O Maryam, how is this to you? She said: It is from Allah' [Al Imran 37]. This indicates that that was not a miracle for Zakariyya (peace be upon him). Fourth: He, exalted, mentioned these breaking-of-customs events in the context of glorifying the state of Maryam, and did not mention with them anything indicating making them a miracle for any of the prophets. And if the purpose had been to manifest the verification of Zakariyya rather than manifesting the honor of Maryam, then mentioning Zakariyya upon mentioning these breaking-of-customs events would have been more appropriate than mentioning Maryam. Since the matter is not so, we know that the purpose was to honor Maryam, not to verify Zakariyya.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage defines the issue of karamat al-awliya (miracles of saints) and presents the first of three proofs. Imam Razi begins by stating the disagreement: the Mu'tazila deny karamat, and the Ash'ari scholar Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini agreed with them, but most Ash'aris affirm them, as did the Mu'tazili Abu al-Husayn al-Basri. Then he gives his first proof: the story of Maryam. She conceived without a male and received sustenance without apparent cause—these are khariq al-'adah (breaks in custom). She was not a prophet, so these events must be karamat of a saint. He then anticipates an objection: perhaps these were mu'jizat (prophetic miracles) for Zakariyya (her guardian). Razi refutes this with four points: (1) A miracle must be manifest to deniers, but Maryam's pregnancy was private. (2) A miracle requires the prophet to be present, but Zakariyya was not present when the events occurred, as shown by the verse commanding Maryam to speak to no one. (3) A miracle should be requested by the prophet, but Zakariyya was unaware of how these things happened, as shown by his question 'How is this to you?' (4) The Quran mentions these events to honor Maryam, not to verify Zakariyya. Thus, they are karamat.

Key Terms
كرامات الأولياء
خوارق العادات
معجزة
المعتزلة
الأستاذ أبو إسحاق
زكريا
مريم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think any supernatural event must be a prophetic miracle.
Correction: Razi shows that when a supernatural event occurs for a non-prophet, it is a karamah, not a mu'jizah.

Checkpoint
What is the main purpose of Imam Razi's first proof regarding Maryam?
A) To prove that Maryam was a prophet
B) To argue that the events of Maryam are karamat of a saint
C) To show that Zakariyya performed miracles
D) To deny the possibility of karamat

Key Takeaway
The story of Maryam provides a clear example of karamat because she was not a prophet, and the Quran’s context shows it honors her, not Zakariyya.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you receive a blessing from Allah, do you tend to attribute it to the 'prophet' (the scholar, the leader) rather than seeing it as God's direct honor to you? How does Razi's argument that Maryam's miracles were for her honor, not for Zakariyya, challenge your reliance on intermediaries?
?
Maryam's miracles were hidden—no one saw the pregnancy or the provision except her. Are you willing to serve Allah in secret, without public validation, trusting that He honors you even when no one else knows? What holds you back?
?
Razi refutes the idea that a miracle must be requested by a prophet. When you face trials, do you bargain with Allah for signs on your terms, or do you submit to His hidden ways as Maryam did? What would it mean for you to trust Allah's provision without demanding a visible cause?

Second Proof: The Companions of the Cave as a Saintly Miracle
Imam Razi argues the miracle of Ashab al-Kahf is a karamah, not a prophetic miracle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses the story of Ashab al-Kahf to argue for the existence of saintly miracles (karamat) by distinguishing them from prophetic miracles.

Core Idea
That which is hidden and unknowable cannot serve as a prophetic miracle, so the Cave event must be a karamah.

Imam Razi presents his second proof for karamat by examining the extraordinary sleep of the Companions of the Cave. He anticipates and refutes the objection that this could be a prophetic miracle for an unknown prophet, using two key criteria: publicness and knowability. This lesson walks through his careful reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim and the proof
Read the passage carefully to grasp Razi's initial argument: the long sleep of non-prophet Companions must be a karamah.
Note that Razi assumes the event is miraculous.
2
Identify the objection
Find the objection that this might be a miracle for an unknown prophet. Razi labels it as a potential counterargument.
This objection is common when distinguishing prophetic and saintly miracles.
3
Analyze the first refutation
Razi argues that prophetic miracles are public, but the youths concealed themselves. This contradicts the nature of a prophetic miracle.
The verse quote 'Let no one be aware of you' is key.
4
Analyze the second refutation
Razi argues that a prophetic miracle must be knowable to serve as evidence, but the duration of the sleep could not be known by observers.
Unknowability disqualifies it as a prophetic sign.
5
Conclude the reasoning
Since both refutations show it cannot be a prophetic miracle, it must be a karamah. Ensure you follow the logical flow.
This strengthens the case for saintly miracles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : أن الله تعالى أبقى أصحاب الكهف ثلاث مائة سنة وأزيد في النوم أحياء من غير آفة وهم ما كانوا من الأنبياء، فوجب أن يكون هذا من باب الكرامات. فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن ذلك معجزة لبعض الأنبياء، وإن كنا ما عرفناهم؟ قلنا هذا باطل من وجهين : الأول: أن تلك الأحوال لو كانت معجزة للأنبياء لما جاز إخفاؤها، لكنهم اجتهدوا في إخفائها، حيث قالوا: ﴿وَلَا يُشْعِرَنَ بِكُمْ أَحَدًا﴾ . والثاني : هو أن بقاء قوم مدة ثلاث مائة سنة أحياء لا يمكن أن يصير معلوما للخلق، وما لا يمكن أن يصير معلومًا للخلق لا يمكن جعله معجزة دالة على صدق مدعي النبوة، فثبت أن هذا لا يصلح أن يكون معجزة، فلم يبق إلا أن يكون كرامة.
The second proof: that Allah, exalted, kept the Companions of the Cave for three hundred years and more in sleep alive without harm, and they were not from among the prophets, so it is necessary that this is from the category of miracles (of saints). If it is said: Why is it not permissible to say that that was a miracle for some prophets, even if we do not know them? We say: This is false from two aspects: First: that those states if they were a miracle for prophets, it would not be permissible to conceal them, but they endeavored to conceal them, as they said: 'And let no one be aware of you' [Kahf 19]. Second: that the survival of a people for a duration of three hundred years alive cannot become known to creation, and what cannot become known to creation cannot be made a miracle indicating the truthfulness of a claimant of prophethood. So it is established that this cannot be a miracle, so nothing remains but that it is a miracle (of a saint).
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage presents Imam Razi's second proof for karamat: the story of Ashab al-Kahf (the Companions of the Cave). They slept for over three hundred years alive, unharmed, and were not prophets. Therefore, this must be a karamah (saintly miracle). He anticipates an objection: perhaps this was a miracle for an unknown prophet. Razi refutes this with two arguments: (1) Prophetic miracles are meant to be public evidence, so they should not be concealed. But the youths actively tried to hide themselves, saying 'Let no one be aware of you.' (2) A prophetic miracle must be knowable to people to serve as proof of prophethood. The prolonged sleep of the cave dwellers was inherently hidden—no ordinary human could observe them continuously and certify its duration. Thus, it cannot function as a prophetic miracle. Hence, it must be a karamah.

Key Terms
أصحاب الكهف
كرامة
معجزة
إخفاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The event of Ashab al-Kahf could have been a miracle for an unknown prophet.
Correction: Razi shows that prophetic miracles must be public and knowable, but this event was hidden and unknowable, so it cannot be a prophetic miracle.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi argue that the event of Ashab al-Kahf cannot be a prophetic miracle?
A) Because it lasted too long
B) Because the youths were not prophets and they concealed themselves, and the event was unknowable
C) Because it happened before Islam
D) Because the Quran does not mention it as a miracle

Key Takeaway
The story of Ashab al-Kahf, being hidden and unknowable, cannot serve as a prophetic miracle but is a clear example of a saintly miracle (karamah).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Companions of the Cave deliberately hid their miracle from others. Have you ever received a spiritual blessing or answered du'a that you chose to keep completely private? Why did you hide it, and what does that choice reveal about your intention? Could you be content if Allah never allowed anyone to see the good He does through you?
?
Razi argues that prophetic miracles must be public to serve as proof. If Allah were to grant you a clear sign of His favor, would you seek to make it known as a testimony, or would you fear the attention? What does your instinct tell you about your attachment to people's recognition versus Allah's alone?
?
The 300-year sleep of the Cave dwellers remained unknown for centuries, yet it was a real miracle from Allah. Does your faith require external validation—wanting others to see your devotion or spiritual experiences? How would your relationship with Allah change if every act of worship you performed remained entirely unseen and unacknowledged forever?

Third Proof: Analogy from Spiritual to Material Gifts
Imam Razi argues from greater to lesser: spiritual gifts are greater than material ones.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's a fortiori argument for the possibility of karamat (extraordinary material favors) based on the greater spiritual favors already granted by Allah to His saints.

Core Idea
If Allah grants spiritual gifts, material ones are even more plausible.

Imam Razi presents his third proof for karamat using a simple yet powerful argument from greater to lesser (qiyas al-awlawiyya). He observes that Allah honors His saints with the greatest gifts of all—knowledge and love of Him—so why would lesser material provisions be considered improbable? This passage challenges the notion that material miracles are somehow more remarkable than spiritual ones.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage in Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic sentence once, then the translation. Notice the key terms: تشريف (honoring), معرفة (knowledge), محبة (love), رغيف (loaf), مفازة (desert).
The passage is a single concise argument.
2
Identify the logical structure: a fortiori
Imam Razi uses an argument from greater to lesser: if the greater (spiritual honors) is true, the lesser (material provisions) is even more likely. Identify the greater and the lesser in the text.
This is known as qiyas al-awlawiyya in Islamic logic.
3
Reflect on the implication for karamat
Consider how this argument supports the possibility of karamat. The commentary explains that material miracles are less remarkable than spiritual ones, so they should not be considered impossible.
This connects to the broader debate about saintly miracles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : أن تشريف الله تعالى عبده بمعرفته ومحبته أعظم وأعلى من إعطائه رغيفا في المفازة أو سقيه شربة من الماء، وإذا لم يبعد الأول كيف يبعد الثاني؟
The third proof: that Allah's honoring of His servant with knowledge of Him and love of Him is greater and higher than giving him a loaf in the desert or giving him a drink of water, and if the first is not far, how can the second be far?
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage presents Imam Razi's third proof for karamat. It is a brief argument from greater to lesser (qiyas al-awlawiyya). He points out that Allah honors His saint with ma'rifah (knowledge) and mahabbah (love) of Him—this is the greatest gift. If that spiritual honor is not considered improbable, then how can one consider improbable a lesser, material gift like a loaf of bread in the desert or a drink of water? The implication is that since saints already receive extraordinary spiritual favors, extraordinary material favors (karamat) are not impossible.

Correction: The passage shows that material miracles are less remarkable than spiritual honors, so their possibility follows logically.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi uses an a fortiori argument to show that if Allah grants spiritual honors like knowledge and love, material provisions like food are even more plausible, thus supporting the possibility of karamat.

?
If you believe that Allah can give you profound spiritual insight and love, what is stopping you from trusting Him completely for your next meal, your health, or your future? What do you need to change in your heart today so that your trust matches your belief?

Three Objections of Deniers Against Karamat
Imam Razi enumerates three objections from deniers of karamat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three objections raised by those who deny the possibility of karamat (saintly miracles) and the reasoning behind each.

Core Idea
Deniers argue that karamat would lead to skepticism, undermine prophetic evidence, and blur the line between prophets and saints.

In this passage, Imam Razi presents the arguments of those who reject the concept of karamat. They offer three logical proofs to show why allowing miracles for saints leads to unacceptable consequences. These objections must be understood before responding to them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Note that the third objection is cut off, but you can infer its direction.
The passage ends mid-sentence. The third objection likely extends the logic of the second.
2
Identify the three objections
The deniers present three distinct proofs. Find each one and note its main claim.
The first two are complete; the third is truncated.
3
Understand the first objection
The first objection argues that if karamat are possible, any improbable event could be claimed as a miracle, leading to complete skepticism (safsatah).
This is a slippery slope argument.
4
Understand the second objection
The second objection states that prophetic miracles (mu'jizat) serve as evidence for prophethood. If similar events occur for saints, they lose their evidential value.
This relies on the condition that evidence must be exclusive.
5
Consider the third objection's implications
Although incomplete, the third objection likely warns that if karamat are permitted for some saints, they could appear for anyone, blurring the distinction between prophets and saints.
Think about how this would challenge the unique status of prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج المنكرون بوجوه : الحجة الأولى: أن تجويز الكرامات يفضي إلى القول بالسفسطة ؛ لأنا إذا جوزنا الكرامات فلعل الله تعالى قلب الجبل ياقوتا كرامة لبعض الأولياء، ولعله جفف البحر كرامة لولي ثانٍ ولعله خلق هذا الإنسان الشيخ في هذه الساعة كرامة لولي ثالث، وقس عليه، ومعلوم أن تجويزه يفضي على الجهالات. الحجة الثانية من شرط الدليل أنه أينما حصل لا بد أن يحصل معه المدلول، وظهور الخوارق يدل على النبوة، فلو حصل في حق النبي وفي حق غيره لخرج عن كونه دالا على صدق النبي وذلك باطل بالإجماع. الحجة الثالثة : لو جاز ظهور الخوارق في حق بعض الأولياء لجاز ظهوره في حق
And the deniers argued with proofs: The first proof: that permitting the miracles of saints leads to skepticism; because if we permit the miracles of saints, perhaps Allah transforms a mountain into a ruby as a miracle for some saint, and perhaps He dries up the sea as a miracle for a second saint, and perhaps He creates this elderly human being at this hour as a miracle for a third saint, and so on, and it is known that permitting it leads to ignorances. The second proof: one of the conditions of evidence is that wherever it obtains, the thing evidenced must obtain, and the appearance of breaking-of-customs events indicates prophethood, so if it occurs in the right of a prophet and in the right of someone else, it would exit from being an indicator of the truthfulness of the prophet, and that is false by consensus. The third proof: if the appearance of breaking-of-customs events is permitted in the right of some saints, then its appearance in the right of
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage presents the arguments of those who deny karamat (the deniers). They offer three proofs. The first objection: if karamat are allowed, then we must admit that Allah could arbitrarily transform a mountain into a ruby, dry up the sea, or create an old man instantaneously—each as a karamah for different saints. This leads to skepticism (safsatah) because anything could be claimed, and we would lose certainty about the natural order. The second objection: the defining feature of a prophetic miracle (mu'jizah) is that it serves as evidence (dalil) confirming truthfulness. For evidence, if the sign (the breaking of custom) occurs in the case of both the prophet and a non-prophet, it ceases to be reliable evidence. Since consensus holds that prophetic miracles are distinctive, karamat would undermine this distinctiveness. The third objection is truncated: if karamat are allowed for some saints, then they could appear in the right of anyone, potentially blurring the line between prophets and saints. The text ends mid-sentence, but the argument is that karamat would destroy the unique evidential value of prophetic miracles.

Key Terms
السفسطة
شرط الدليل
المدلول
الخوارق
الإجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the deniers' arguments are weak or easily dismissed.
Correction: These are serious logical challenges. Scholars had to carefully define karamat to avoid these pitfalls, showing the depth of Islamic theological reasoning.

Checkpoint
What is the core concern of the first objection brought by the deniers of karamat?
A) That karamat would make prophets unnecessary
B) That allowing karamat would lead to skepticism about all events
C) That karamat would make saints equal to prophets
D) That karamat are impossible because they break physical laws

Key Takeaway
Deniers use strong logical arguments against karamat, focusing on epistemological and evidential concerns that must be addressed by defenders of saintly miracles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear of a miraculous event attributed to a saint or a pious person, does it strengthen your faith or create doubt? Why? Reflect on the deniers' concern about skepticism: is there any part of your belief that would be shaken if you could not distinguish between prophetic and saintly miracles?
?
The deniers argue that evidence for prophethood must be unique. In your own search for truth, do you demand unique, unmistakable signs? Are there areas where you accept weaker evidence, and what does that say about your conviction?
?
If you were to witness a clear karamah performed by a known saint, would you feel compelled to follow that person as a prophet? How does this lesson's discussion of the boundary between prophet and saint challenge your assumptions about spiritual authority?

The Proof from Maryam's Karamat
Razi's first proof from the story of Maryam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's first scriptural argument for the reality of karamat based on the story of Maryam.

Core Idea
Extraordinary events can honor saints, not just confirm prophets.

Razi begins his case for karamat by examining the Quranic account of Maryam. He argues that the miraculous provision at her prayer niche was mentioned to honor her, not as a miracle for Zakariyya. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical structure: premise, conditional, conclusion.
The passage uses a hypothetical reasoning: if the purpose were X, then Y would occur. Since Y did not occur, X is false.
2
Identify the key elements
Identify the extraordinary events mentioned for Maryam, and note the absence of Zakariyya's mention alongside them.
This contrast is central to the argument.
3
Understand the conclusion
Why does Razi conclude that the events are a karamat? Connect the absence of Zakariyya to the purpose of honoring Mary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رايعها : أنه تعالى ذكر هذه الخوارق في معرض تعظيم حال مريم ولم يذكر معها ما شعر بجعلها معجزة لأحد من الأنبياء، ولو كان المقصود منها إظهار تصديق زكريا لا إظهار كرامة مريم لكان ذكر زكريا عند ذكر هذه الخوارق أولى من ذكر مريم . ولما لم يكن الأمر كذلك علمنا أن المقصود منها إكرام مريم لا تصديق زكريا. الحجة الثانية : أن الله تعالى أبقى أصحاب الكهف ثلاث مائة سنة وأزيد في النوم أحياء من غير ،آفة وهم ما كانوا من الأنبياء، فوجب أن يكون هذا من باب الكرامات. فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن ذلك معجزة لبعض الأنبياء، وإن كنا ما عرفناهم؟ قلنا هذا باطل من وجهين : الأول: أن تلك الأحوال لو كانت معجزة للأنبياء لما جاز إخفاؤها، لكنهم اجتهدوا في إخفائها، حيث قالوا: ﴿وَلَا يُشْعِرَنَ بِكُمْ أَحَدًا ﴾ [الكهف: ١٩] . والثاني : هو أن بقاء قوم مدة ثلاث مائة سنة أحياء لا يمكن أن يصير معلوما للخلق، وما لا يمكن أن يصير معلومًا للخلق لا يمكن جعله معجزة دالة على صدق مدعي النبوة، فثبت أن هذا لا يصلح أن يكون معجزة، فلم يبق إلا أن يكون كرامة. الحجة الثالثة : أن تشريف الله تعالى عبده بمعرفته ومحبته أعظم وأعلى من إعطائه رغيفا في المفازة أو سقيه شربة من الماء، وإذا لالم يبعد الأول كيف يبعد الثاني ؟
Its fourth: that God Almighty mentioned these extraordinary events in the context of magnifying the status of Mary, and He did not mention with them what indicates that they were a miracle for any of the prophets. And if the purpose had been to manifest the confirmation of Zakariyya, not to manifest the honor of Mary, then mentioning Zakariyya when mentioning these extraordinary events would have been more appropriate than mentioning Mary. And since the matter was not so, we know that the purpose was to honor Mary, not to confirm Zakariyya. The second proof: that God Almighty preserved the Companions of the Cave for three hundred years and more in sleep, alive without affliction, and they were not among the prophets, so it is necessary that this be from the category of karamat. If it is said: why is it not permissible to say that that was a miracle for some prophets, even if we do not know them? We say this is false from two aspects: first: that those states, if they were a miracle for the prophets, would not have been permissible to conceal, but they strove to conceal them, when they said: 'And let no one know of you' (Quran 18:19). Second: that the survival of a group for three hundred years alive cannot become known to people, and what cannot become known to people cannot be made a miracle indicating the truthfulness of a claimant of prophethood. So it is established that this cannot be a miracle, so nothing remains except that it be a karamat. The third proof: that God's honoring His servant with His knowledge and love is greater and higher than giving him a loaf of bread in the desert or giving him a drink of water. And if the former is not unlikely, how can the latter be unlikely?
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments in favor of the reality of karamat (extraordinary events that occur to saints, as distinct from prophetic miracles). The first argument (labeled 'its fourth,' indicating it is the fourth in a sequence) draws from the Quranic story of Maryam (Mary). Razi observes that God mentioned the extraordinary events at her prayer niche in the context of honoring her, not as a miracle for Zakariyya (Zechariah). If the purpose had been to confirm Zakariyya's prophethood, Zakariyya would have been mentioned explicitly. Since he was not, the events are an honor for Mary, i.e., a karamat. The second argument cites the Companions of the Cave (Ashab al-Kahf), who slept for over 300 years without harm and were not prophets; thus their preservation must be a karamat. Razi then addresses an objection: perhaps this was a miracle for an unknown prophet. He refutes it with two points: (1) the Companions hid themselves, whereas a prophetic miracle must be public to serve as proof; (2) the event was not observable by others, so it cannot function as a miracle that confirms prophethood. Therefore it remains a karamat. The third argument is a logical analogy: if God can honor a servant with the greater gifts of knowledge and love of Him, then the lesser material favors (like food and drink in need) are even more plausible as karamat. Thus the occurrence of such favors is not unlikely.

Key Terms
كرامة
معجزة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any extraordinary event in the Quran must be a prophetic miracle.
Correction: Razi shows that the events at Maryam's prayer niche were for her honor, not as a miracle for a prophet.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the proof from the story of Maryam?
A) She was a prophet.
B) Her miracles were actually for Zakariyya.
C) Her extraordinary events were an honor for her, not a miracle for Zakariyya.
D) She performed miracles to prove her prophethood.

Key Takeaway
Razi's first proof shows that extraordinary events can be karamat honoring saints, not only prophetic miracles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran, do you quickly assume that every extraordinary event is a miracle for a prophet? How might this assumption cause you to miss the honor God gives to righteous believers like Maryam?
?
Maryam was honored by God with extraordinary provision. Do you believe that God could honor you in ways that defy normal expectations? What would it take for you to truly expect karamat in your own life?
?
If you were in Maryam's position, would you recognize God's favor as a personal honor, or would you attribute it to someone else? Are you currently attributing God's gifts in your life to others instead of seeing His direct care for you?

The Proof from the Companions of the Cave
Razi's second proof from Ashab al-Kahf with objection and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second argument for karamat using the story of Ashab al-Kahf and the refutation of the objection.

Core Idea
A prophetic miracle must be public, but the Companions' concealment proves their preservation was a karamat.

The second proof draws on the famous story of the sleepers in the cave. Razi anticipates an objection that it might be a miracle for an unknown prophet and offers two decisive refutations. This lesson walks through the argument and its defense.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Identify the main claim and the objection.
2
Identify the two refutations
List the two reasons Razi gives to refute the objection.
One is about concealment, the other about public knowledge.
3
Connect to the conclusion
How do these refutations support the claim that the event is a karamat?
Because it cannot be a miracle, it must be a karamat.

Its fourth: that God Almighty mentioned these extraordinary events in the context of magnifying the status of Mary, and He did not mention with them what indicates that they were a miracle for any of the prophets. And if the purpose had been to manifest the confirmation of Zakariyya, not to manifest the honor of Mary, then mentioning Zakariyya when mentioning these extraordinary events would have been more appropriate than mentioning Mary. And since the matter was not so, we know that the purpose was to honor Mary, not to confirm Zakariyya. Second: that the survival of a group for three hundred years alive cannot become known to people, and what cannot become known to people cannot be made a miracle indicating the truthfulness of a claimant of prophethood. So it is established that this cannot be a miracle, so nothing remains except that it be a karamat. The third proof: that God's honoring His servant with His knowledge and love is greater and higher than giving him a loaf of bread in the desert or giving him a drink of water. And if the former is not unlikely, how can the latter be unlikely?
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments in favor of the reality of karamat (extraordinary events that occur to saints, as distinct from prophetic miracles). The first argument (labeled 'its fourth,' indicating it is the fourth in a sequence) draws from the Quranic story of Maryam (Mary). Razi observes that God mentioned the extraordinary events at her prayer niche in the context of honoring her, not as a miracle for Zakariyya (Zechariah). If the purpose had been to confirm Zakariyya's prophethood, Zakariyya would have been mentioned explicitly. Since he was not, the events are an honor for Mary, i.e., a karamat. The second argument cites the Companions of the Cave (Ashab al-Kahf), who slept for over 300 years without harm and were not prophets; thus their preservation must be a karamat. Razi then addresses an objection: perhaps this was a miracle for an unknown prophet. He refutes it with two points: (1) the Companions hid themselves, whereas a prophetic miracle must be public to serve as proof; (2) the event was not observable by others, so it cannot function as a miracle that confirms prophethood. Therefore it remains a karamat. The third argument is a logical analogy: if God can honor a servant with the greater gifts of knowledge and love of Him, then the lesser material favors (like food and drink in need) are even more plausible as karamat. Thus the occurrence of such favors is not unlikely.

Key Terms
كرامة
معجزة
دعوى النبوة
أصحاب الكهف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any extraordinary event must be a prophetic miracle, even if it is not public.
Correction: Razi shows that a miracle must be public and observable; otherwise, it is a karamat.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi say the event of the Companions of the Cave cannot be a prophetic miracle?
A) Because the Companions were not prophets.
B) Because the event was concealed and not publicly observable.
C) Because it lasted too long.
D) Because it was mentioned in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
Razi's second proof shows that an extraordinary event that is concealed and unobservable cannot be a prophetic miracle, so it must be a karamat.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever concealed a good deed or a spiritual experience out of humility, like the Companions of the Cave? When you do so, what is your intention—is it for Allah or for people? What does this lesson reveal about the value of hidden acts of righteousness?
?
The objection in this lesson comes from the possibility that the event could be a miracle for an unknown prophet. This shows that people can always find excuses to deny clear proofs. Where in your own life do you find yourself making excuses to avoid submitting to Allah's signs? What would it take for you to accept a proof without reservation?

The Proof from the Greater Honor
Razi's third proof by analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's third rational argument for karamat, based on analogy with God's greater gifts.

Core Idea
If God grants the greater honor of knowledge and love, the lesser material favors are even more plausible.

The third proof is a simple but powerful analogy: the spiritual honor God bestows on His saints is far greater than any material provision. If the greater is possible, the lesser is even more so. This lesson examines the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the comparison between spiritual honor and material provision.
2
Analyze the analogy
Identify the 'greater' and the 'lesser' in Razi's argument. Then reflect on why the conclusion follows.
The argument is: if the greater is plausible, the lesser is even more plausible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رايعها : أنه تعالى ذكر هذه الخوارق في معرض تعظيم حال مريم ولم يذكر معها ما شعر بجعلها معجزة لأحد من الأنبياء، ولو كان المقصود منها إظهار تصديق زكريا لا إظهار كرامة مريم لكان ذكر زكريا عند ذكر هذه الخوارق أولى من ذكر مريم . ولما لم يكن الأمر كذلك علمنا أن المقصود منها إكرام مريم لا تصديق زكريا. الحجة الثانية : أن الله تعالى أبقى أصحاب الكهف ثلاث مائة سنة وأزيد في النوم أحياء من غير ،آفة وهم ما كانوا من الأنبياء، فوجب أن يكون هذا من باب الكرامات. فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن ذلك معجزة لبعض الأنبياء، وإن كنا ما عرفناهم؟ قلنا هذا باطل من وجهين : الأول: أن تلك الأحوال لو كانت معجزة للأنبياء لما جاز إخفاؤها، لكنهم اجتهدوا في إخفائها، حيث قالوا: ﴿وَلَا يُشْعِرَنَ بِكُمْ أَحَدًا ﴾ [الكهف: ١٩] . والثاني : هو أن بقاء قوم مدة ثلاث مائة سنة أحياء لا يمكن أن يصير معلوما للخلق، وما لا يمكن أن يصير معلومًا للخلق لا يمكن جعله معجزة دالة على صدق مدعي النبوة، فثبت أن هذا لا يصلح أن يكون معجزة، فلم يبق إلا أن يكون كرامة. الحجة الثالثة : أن تشريف الله تعالى عبده بمعرفته ومحبته أعظم وأعلى من إعطائه رغيفا في المفازة أو سقيه شربة من الماء، وإذا لالم يبعد الأول كيف يبعد الثاني ؟
Its fourth: that God Almighty mentioned these extraordinary events in the context of magnifying the status of Mary, and He did not mention with them what indicates that they were a miracle for any of the prophets. And if the purpose had been to manifest the confirmation of Zakariyya, not to manifest the honor of Mary, then mentioning Zakariyya when mentioning these extraordinary events would have been more appropriate than mentioning Mary. And since the matter was not so, we know that the purpose was to honor Mary, not to confirm Zakariyya. The second proof: that God Almighty preserved the Companions of the Cave for three hundred years and more in sleep, alive without affliction, and they were not among the prophets, so it is necessary that this be from the category of karamat. If it is said: why is it not permissible to say that that was a miracle for some prophets, even if we do not know them? We say this is false from two aspects: first: that those states, if they were a miracle for the prophets, would not have been permissible to conceal, but they strove to conceal them, when they said: 'And let no one know of you' (Quran 18:19). Second: that the survival of a group for three hundred years alive cannot become known to people, and what cannot become known to people cannot be made a miracle indicating the truthfulness of a claimant of prophethood. So it is established that this cannot be a miracle, so nothing remains except that it be a karamat. The third proof: that God's honoring His servant with His knowledge and love is greater and higher than giving him a loaf of bread in the desert or giving him a drink of water. And if the former is not unlikely, how can the latter be unlikely?

Five Objections Against Karamat
Razi enumerates five arguments of those who deny karamat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five main rational objections raised against the possibility of karamat, and the logical structure behind each one.

Core Idea
Five objections aim to show that karamat would undermine the very concept of miracles and proofs.

Before Razi defends the possibility of karamat, he first presents the strongest arguments of those who deny them. These five objections are not trivial — they force us to think carefully about how miracles function as proofs and what distinguishes a prophet from a saint. Understanding these objections is essential for grasping the later defense.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through. Then read the English translation. Notice that the passage lists five distinct arguments, each beginning with 'الحجة'.
The passage is dense with technical terms like سفسطة and دليل.
2
Identify the five arguments
Write down the five arguments in order. For each, identify the core claim and the reasoning behind it.
The arguments are numbered in the text itself.
3
Understand the first argument: sophistry
The first argument warns that allowing karamat leads to radical skepticism. Imagine any arbitrary miracle could happen — how would we know anything? Grasp the idea that if anything can happen, knowledge becomes impossible.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
4
Analyze arguments two and three: proof and exclusivity
The second argument focuses on the nature of a proof (دليل): if miracles occur to both prophets and saints, they lose their power to prove prophethood. The third extends this: if any saint can break norms, then norm-breaking no longer signifies anything special.
These are the most epistemologically weighty objections.
5
Reflect on arguments four and five: prestige and legal conflict
The fourth argument says miracles confer prestige, which must be exclusive to prophets. The fifth creates a legal dilemma: a saint cannot be questioned (due to his rank) but the law requires evidence — contradiction.
Notice the shift from epistemology to social and legal reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج المنكرون بوجوه : الحجة الأولى: أن تجويز الكرامات يفضي إلى القول بالسفسطة ؛ لأنا إذا جوزنا الكرامات فلعل الله تعالى قلب الجبل ياقوتا كرامة لبعض الأولياء، ولعله جفف البحر كرامة لولي ثانٍ ولعله خلق هذا الإنسان الشيخ في هذه الساعة كرامة لولي ثالث، وقس عليه، ومعلوم أن تجويزه يفضي على الجهالات. الحجة الثانية من شرط الدليل أنه أينما حصل لا بد أن يحصل معه المدلول، وظهور الخوارق يدل على النبوة، فلو حصل في حق النبي الله وفي حق غيره لخرج عن كونه دالا على صدق النبي وذلك باطل بالإجماع. الحجة الثالثة : لو جاز ظهور الخوارق في حق بعض الأولياء لجاز ظهوره في حق الباقين، وعلى هذا التقدير يخرج انخراق هذه العادات عن كونه دليلا على النبوة، وعن كونه دليلا على الكرامة. الحجة الرابعة : لو ظهرت هذه الخوارق على النبي وعلى غيره لم يبق لظهورها وقع ولا ،مرتبة كما أن المنصب الذي يصل إليه الشريف والخسيس فإنه لا يبقى له وقع، ولما اتفق العقلاء على أن ظهور هذه الخوارق منصب عظيم علمنا أنه لابد أن يكون مخصوصا بالأنبياء - عليهم السلام. الحجة الخامسة : لو ظهرت الكرامات على إنسان ثم ادعى ذلك الإنسان على أحد مالا فإما أن يطالبه بالبينة أو لا يطالب لا جائز أن يطالبه ؛ لأنه بتلك الكرامة كمال حاله ودرجته عند الله ومتى كان كذلك امتنع أن يكون في ادعائه المال على الغير كاذبًا، ولا يجوز أن لا يطالب بالبينة لقوله الا الله : «البيئة على المدعي واليمين على من أنكر ولما بطل القسمان علمنا أنه لا يجوز ظهور الكرامة.
And the deniers argued with several points: The first proof: that permitting karamat leads to the doctrine of sophistry; because if we permit karamat, then perhaps God Almighty turns the mountain into ruby as a karamat for some saint, and perhaps He dries up the sea as a karamat for a second saint, and perhaps He creates this elderly person at this hour as a karamat for a third saint, and so on. And it is known that permitting this leads to absurdities. The second proof: from the condition of the proof is that wherever it occurs, the proven must occur with it; and the appearance of extraordinary events indicates prophethood. So if it occurs in the case of the Prophet ﷺ and in the case of others, it would cease to be indicative of the truthfulness of the prophet, and that is invalid by consensus. The third proof: if the appearance of extraordinary events is permitted in the case of some saints, then their appearance is permitted in the case of the rest, and on this basis the breaking of these norms would cease to be a proof of prophethood and a proof of karamat. The fourth proof: if these extraordinary events appear upon the prophet and upon others, their appearance would retain no prestige or rank, just as a rank that is attained by both the noble and the base has no prestige. And since the rational people agree that the appearance of these extraordinary events is a great rank, we know that it must be specific to the prophets, peace be upon them. The fifth proof: if karamat appear on a person, and then that person claims money from someone, then either he is asked to provide evidence or he is not. It is not permissible that he be asked to provide evidence, because by that karamat he has attained perfection of his state and rank with God, and when such is the case, it is impossible for him to be lying in his claim of money from another. And it is not permissible that he not be required to provide evidence because of his statement (peace be upon him): 'Evidence is upon the claimant, and the oath is upon the one who denies.' And when both divisions are invalid, we know that the appearance of karamat is not permissible.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage lists five arguments raised by those who deny the possibility of karamat (extraordinary events occurring to saints). The first argument uses a reductio ad absurdum: if karamat are allowed, then any arbitrary miracle could occur—like mountains turning to ruby or seas drying up—and such a world would be subject to sophistry, making knowledge uncertain. The second argument is based on the nature of a proof: a proof must reliably indicate what it proves. If prophetic miracles also occur to non-prophets, they cease to be a reliable sign of prophethood. The third argument extends this: if some saints can perform karamat, then all saints could, and the category of breaking natural laws would no longer serve as a distinguishing sign for either prophethood or sainthood. The fourth argument appeals to the prestige of miracles: if both prophets and non-prophets perform them, their special status is diminished, akin to a high office that loses prestige when attainable by the low-born. The fifth argument is practical: if a saint performs a karamat and then claims money from someone, he cannot be required to provide evidence (since his high rank precludes falsehood) nor can he be excused from providing evidence (because the Prophet ﷺ commanded evidence for claimants); thus the situation is contradictory, so karamat cannot occur.

Key Terms
سفسطة
دليل
مدلول
انخراق العادات
بينة
يمين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi himself denies karamat because he presents these objections without refuting them here.
Correction: Razi is presenting the opponents' arguments; later he will refute each one. The passage is a summary of the view he intends to critique.

Checkpoint
According to the second objection, why would karamat undermine the proof of prophethood?
A) Because it would create a competing claim to prophethood.
B) Because if both prophets and saints can perform miracles, then miracles cannot distinguish a true prophet.
C) Because saints are more virtuous than prophets.
D) Because the claim of prophethood would become invalid.

Key Takeaway
The deniers of karamat argue that allowing miracles for saints would destroy the epistemic, social, and legal functions of miracles, making them useless as proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a claim that seems extraordinary, do you first seek evidence or are you inclined to accept it based on the person's reputation? How does this lesson challenge your default response?
?
Imagine you witnessed something that appears to be a miracle from a saint. Would your certainty about their truthfulness extend to every claim they make, even in matters of money? What caution does the fifth objection place on your heart?
?
If the proof of prophethood is miracles, and you believe in the Prophet ﷺ, how does your understanding of that proof change when you realize it could be imitated? Are you willing to critically examine what you consider signs from Allah, even if it feels uncomfortable?

Response to the First Objection: Refuting the Denier of Karamat
Razi answers the first objection by showing it is universal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's method of turning the objection back on the denier by showing that the problem applies to all philosophical positions.

Core Idea
The objection against karamat is not unique; it applies to everyone.

Razi begins his defense of karamat by addressing the charge that accepting karamat leads to sophistry. He shows that the very problem posed by the objector is also incumbent upon them, regardless of whether they affirm a free-acting agent or deny it. This lesson examines his clever rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice how Razi structures his answer as a refutation by turning the tables.
2
Identify the two worldviews
Razi considers two groups: those who affirm a free-acting agent (God) and those who deny it (naturalists). Identify how each group might explain extraordinary events.
For the first group, God acts without known benefit; for the second, rare celestial configurations cause them.
3
Understand the conclusion
Razi concludes that the problem is necessary upon all groups. Why does this neutralize the objection?
The objection was that karamat lead to uncertainty; but if every worldview faces the same possibility, it is not a special objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى: أن منكر الكرامة يلزمه ذلك الإشكال لأنه أثبت للعالم فاعلا محتارًا، فلعل ذلك الفاعل فعل هذه الأمور من قلب الجبال ذهبًا وقلب البحار دمًا وما يشبهه إما من غير رعاية مصلحة - عند من لا يوقف فاعلية الله تعالى على رعاية المصلحة - أو لمصلحة لا نعرفها نحن - عند من يوقف فاعلية الله تعالى على رعاية المصالح. وأما من ينكر الفاعل المختار فيلزمه هذا الاحتمال أيضًا لأن سبب حدوث الحوادث في هذا العالم بتشكيلات فلكية واتصالات كوكبية - على مذهبه ـ ولا يبعد أيضًا أن يحدث في الفك تشكل غريب يوجب حدوث هذه الحوادث في هذا العالم فثبت أن هذا الإشكال لازم على كل الفرق. والجواب عن الشبهة الثانية : أن الكرامات والمعجزات - وإن اشتركت في كون كل واحد منهما أمرًا خارقا للعادة - ولكن تمتاز المعجزة عن الكرامة من وجوه : أحدها: أن الدعوى شرط في النبوة وليست شرطا في الكرامة. وثانيها : أن الحاصل في النبوة ادعاء النبوة، وفي الكرامة إما أن لا تحصل الدعوى، أو إن حصلت لكنها لا تكون دعوى النبوة بل دعوى الولاية. ثالثها: أن المعجزة لا تكون لها معارضة والكرامة قد تكون لها معارضة. والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة أن هذا الذي ألزمتموه علينا لازم عليكم في المعجزات، وذلك لأنه تعالى إذا بعث جمعًا كثيرًا من الأنبياء فظهر على يد كل واحد
And the answer to the first doubt: that the denier of karamat is himself obliged by that problem because he has affirmed for the world a free-acting agent. Perhaps that agent did these things—turning mountains into gold and seas into blood and the like—either without regard to benefit (according to those who do not condition God's activity on benefit) or for a benefit we do not know (according to those who condition God's activity on benefits). As for the one who denies the free-acting agent, this possibility also applies to him, because the cause of the occurrence of events in this world, according to his doctrine, is through celestial configurations and planetary conjunctions, and it is not unlikely that a strange configuration occurs in the sphere causing these events in this world. So it is established that this problem is necessary upon all groups. And the answer to the second doubt: that karamat and miracles—even though they share in each being an extraordinary matter—yet the miracle is distinguished from the karamat in several aspects: one of them: that the claim is a condition in prophethood but not a condition in karamat. Second: that what occurs in prophethood is the claim of prophethood, whereas in karamat either no claim occurs, or if it occurs it is not a claim of prophethood but a claim of sainthood. Third: that the miracle cannot have an opposition, whereas the karamat may have an opposition. And the answer to the third doubt: that this which you have obliged upon us is also obligatory upon you concerning miracles, because God Almighty, when He sends a large group of prophets and extraordinary events appear at the hand of each one...
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage contains Razi's responses to the first three objections (shubahat) raised by the deniers of karamat. The answer to the first objection turns the problem of sophistry back on the objector: anyone who affirms a free-acting agent (God) must accept that God could do anything, whether or not it follows a pattern of benefit. Even atheists who deny a free agent face the same issue because their naturalistic system allows for rare celestial configurations that could produce extraordinary events. Thus the problem is not specific to karamat but is a general philosophical issue. The answer to the second objection provides three distinguishing features between a prophetic miracle (mu'jiza) and a saintly miracle (karamat): (1) a claim of prophethood is required for a mu'jiza, not for a karamat; (2) the content of the claim differs—prophethood vs. sainthood; (3) a mu'jiza cannot be opposed (e.g., by another miracle), whereas a karamat may be opposed (i.e., it is not uniquely inimitable). These distinctions show that even though both are extraordinary, they serve different functions and do not confuse the proof of prophethood. The answer to the third objection begins by turning the argument back: the same difficulty of multiple miracles appearing simultaneously from multiple prophets would also apply to the objector's own position. The response is cut off, but the intended point is that the existence of many prophets does not invalidate miracles as proofs, and likewise, the possibility of many saints having karamat does not invalidate the distinct function of prophetic miracles.

Key Terms
فاعل مختار
تشكيلات فلكية
اتصالات كوكبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The problem of sophistry only applies to those who affirm karamat.
Correction: Razi demonstrates that the difficulty is universal, applying even to those who deny a free-acting agent, thus the objection is not decisive against karamat.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why does the first objection apply to both the believer in a free-acting agent and the naturalist?
A) Both affirm that extraordinary events cannot occur.
B) Both must accept the possibility of extraordinary events from their own principles.
C) Both deny the possibility of celestial configurations.
D) Both agree that God must act for a known benefit.

Key Takeaway
Razi's response shows that the objection against karamat is not a special difficulty but a general philosophical problem that all schools face.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear about extraordinary acts of saints (karamat), do you feel a tension between your reason and your faith? Razi argues that doubt is not unique to believers. Will you allow that tension to weaken your trust in God, or will you submit to the fact that God's wisdom may surpass your understanding? Commit today to not let your limited perspective become a barrier to belief.
?
You affirm that God is a free-acting agent. Are you truly comfortable with the possibility that God can do things for reasons you may never grasp? Think of an event in your life that seemed meaningless or unjust. Can you now, in light of this lesson, accept that it might have had a wisdom you do not know? What change in your heart would this acceptance require?
?
The objector's position is shown to be self-defeating. How often do you, in your own thoughts, criticize others for beliefs that you yourself cannot fully explain? Razi teaches that the problem is universal. Will you examine yourself for double standards in your religious judgments? Pray for humility to recognize that your own worldview also has its unanswered questions.
