Distinguishing Karamat from Mu'jizat: Three Criteria
Razi enumerates three differences between mu'jiza and karamat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to articulate the three ways Razi distinguishes a saintly miracle (karamat) from a prophetic miracle (mu'jiza).

Core Idea
A karamat is not a mu'jiza because it lacks a claim and can be opposed.

The second objection conflates karamat with mu'jizat, arguing that if both are extraordinary, then the proof of prophethood collapses. Razi responds by clarifying that they differ in three crucial respects: the presence of a claim, the content of the claim, and the possibility of opposition. This lesson unfolds these distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Read the enumeration
Read the Arabic passage noting the three 'وجوه' (aspects). Compare with the translation to ensure you understand each distinction.
2
Understand the first distinction
A claim of prophethood is a condition for a mu'jiza but not for a karamat. Why does this matter?
Without a claim, an extraordinary event cannot function as a proof of prophecy.
3
Understand the second distinction
Even if a claim is present in a karamat, it is a claim of sainthood, not prophethood. This prevents confusion between the two types.
4
Understand the third distinction
A mu'jiza cannot be opposed (mu'arada), meaning no one else can produce a similar act to challenge it. A karamat may have an opposition. Explore why this is crucial for the uniqueness of prophetic proof.
This distinction ensures that true miracles are inimitable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى: أن منكر الكرامة يلزمه ذلك الإشكال لأنه أثبت للعالم فاعلا محتارًا، فلعل ذلك الفاعل فعل هذه الأمور من قلب الجبال ذهبًا وقلب البحار دمًا وما يشبهه إما من غير رعاية مصلحة - عند من لا يوقف فاعلية الله تعالى على رعاية المصلحة - أو لمصلحة لا نعرفها نحن - عند من يوقف فاعلية الله تعالى على رعاية المصالح. وأما من ينكر الفاعل المختار فيلزمه هذا الاحتمال أيضًا لأن سبب حدوث الحوادث في هذا العالم بتشكيلات فلكية واتصالات كوكبية - على مذهبه ـ ولا يبعد أيضًا أن يحدث في الفك تشكل غريب يوجب حدوث هذه الحوادث في هذا العالم فثبت أن هذا الإشكال لازم على كل الفرق. والجواب عن الشبهة الثانية : أن الكرامات والمعجزات - وإن اشتركت في كون كل واحد منهما أمرًا خارقا للعادة - ولكن تمتاز المعجزة عن الكرامة من وجوه : أحدها: أن الدعوى شرط في النبوة وليست شرطا في الكرامة. وثانيها : أن الحاصل في النبوة ادعاء النبوة، وفي الكرامة إما أن لا تحصل الدعوى، أو إن حصلت لكنها لا تكون دعوى النبوة بل دعوى الولاية. ثالثها: أن المعجزة لا تكون لها معارضة والكرامة قد تكون لها معارضة. والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة أن هذا الذي ألزمتموه علينا لازم عليكم في المعجزات، وذلك لأنه تعالى إذا بعث جمعًا كثيرًا من الأنبياء فظهر على يد كل واحد
And the answer to the first doubt: that the denier of karamat is himself obliged by that problem because he has affirmed for the world a free-acting agent. Perhaps that agent did these things—turning mountains into gold and seas into blood and the like—either without regard to benefit (according to those who do not condition God's activity on benefit) or for a benefit we do not know (according to those who condition God's activity on benefits). As for the one who denies the free-acting agent, this possibility also applies to him, because the cause of the occurrence of events in this world, according to his doctrine, is through celestial configurations and planetary conjunctions, and it is not unlikely that a strange configuration occurs in the sphere causing these events in this world. So it is established that this problem is necessary upon all groups. And the answer to the second doubt: that karamat and miracles—even though they share in each being an extraordinary matter—yet the miracle is distinguished from the karamat in several aspects: one of them: that the claim is a condition in prophethood but not a condition in karamat. Second: that what occurs in prophethood is the claim of prophethood, whereas in karamat either no claim occurs, or if it occurs it is not a claim of prophethood but a claim of sainthood. Third: that the miracle cannot have an opposition, whereas the karamat may have an opposition. And the answer to the third doubt: that this which you have obliged upon us is also obligatory upon you concerning miracles, because God Almighty, when He sends a large group of prophets and extraordinary events appear at the hand of each one...
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage contains Razi's responses to the first three objections (shubahat) raised by the deniers of karamat. The answer to the first objection turns the problem of sophistry back on the objector: anyone who affirms a free-acting agent (God) must accept that God could do anything, whether or not it follows a pattern of benefit. Even atheists who deny a free agent face the same issue because their naturalistic system allows for rare celestial configurations that could produce extraordinary events. Thus the problem is not specific to karamat but is a general philosophical issue. The answer to the second objection provides three distinguishing features between a prophetic miracle (mu'jiza) and a saintly miracle (karamat): (1) a claim of prophethood is required for a mu'jiza, not for a karamat; (2) the content of the claim differs—prophethood vs. sainthood; (3) a mu'jiza cannot be opposed (e.g., by another miracle), whereas a karamat may be opposed (i.e., it is not uniquely inimitable). These distinctions show that even though both are extraordinary, they serve different functions and do not confuse the proof of prophethood. The answer to the third objection begins by turning the argument back: the same difficulty of multiple miracles appearing simultaneously from multiple prophets would also apply to the objector's own position. The response is cut off, but the intended point is that the existence of many prophets does not invalidate miracles as proofs, and likewise, the possibility of many saints having karamat does not invalidate the distinct function of prophetic miracles.

دعوى
ولاية
معارضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any extraordinary event can serve as proof of prophethood.
Correction: Only an extraordinary event accompanied by a claim of prophethood and immune to opposition is a mu'jiza.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is one criterion that distinguishes a mu'jiza from a karamat?
A) A mu'jiza is always material, while a karamat is spiritual.
B) A mu'jiza involves a claim of prophethood, while a karamat does not.
C) A karamat cannot be opposed, while a mu'jiza can.
D) A mu'jiza occurs only to prophets, while a karamat occurs only to saints.

Key Takeaway
The three distinctions show that karamat do not undermine the proof of prophethood because mu'jizat are specifically marked by a claim and uniqueness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever sought dramatic signs or supernatural experiences to confirm your faith, rather than trusting the clear signs of prophethood? What does Razi’s distinction say about your tendency?
?
When you hear someone claim spiritual authority, do you demand evidence that matches the criteria for a prophetic claim, or do you accept extraordinary stories without scrutiny? How will you change?
?
Reflect on a time you felt your own spiritual experience might be a sign of special status. Did you honestly examine whether it could be opposed or lacked the right claim? What does this passage warn you about self-deception?

Response to the Third Objection: The Problem of Multiple Prophets
Razi starts his answer to the third objection by drawing a parallel to multiple prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's strategy of turning the argument about multiple karamat back onto the objector's own position regarding multiple prophets.

Core Idea
The objection based on multiple karamat is the same as the problem of multiple prophets.

The third objection argues that if many saints can perform karamat, then the plurality of extraordinary events would cause confusion. Razi begins his response by noting that the same issue arises with numerous prophets performing miracles. Unfortunately, the passage is cut off, but the direction is clear: the objector's own position faces an analogous difficulty. This lesson explores the beginning of his response.

Guided Path
1
Read the fragment
Read the small Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Razi starts his answer by drawing a parallel to multiple prophets.
The passage is incomplete, so focus on the structure of the rebuttal.
2
Identify the parallel
Razi says that what the objector says about karamat is also necessary for them regarding mu'jizat. Why would the problem be the same?
If many prophets each have miracles, why doesn't that cause confusion like many saints with karamat?
3
Reflect on the incompleteness
Since the passage is cut off, think about how the argument would logically conclude. What would Razi say next?
He likely argues that the same criteria (claim, opposition) that distinguish prophetic miracles from each other also apply to karamat.

The Fourth Objection: Extraordinary Acts Are Exclusive to Prophets
Fourth objection and its refutation by Imam Razi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the fourth objection against karamat (loss of significance) fails when considering the complete definition of miracle.

Core Idea
The proof of prophethood is the complete package: extraordinary act plus claim plus no challenge.

Imam Razi presents the fourth objection that if extraordinary acts appear on both prophets and saints, they lose their significance. But the answer shows that the proof of prophethood is not just any extraordinary act—it is the act combined with the claim of prophethood and the absence of a challenge. This combination is unique to prophets, so karamat do not diminish the rank of prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the fourth objection carefully. Notice how it argues that extraordinary acts must be exclusive to prophets to maintain their significance.
2
Study the answer
Read Razi's answer. He argues that the proof of prophethood is not the act alone but the act plus two conditions: the claim of prophethood and absence of challenge.
This combination does not occur for non-prophets.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this answer preserves the special status of prophethood while allowing karamat for saints.
Karamat do not compete with miracles because they lack the claim of prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : لو ظهرت هذه الخوارق على النبي وعلى غيره لم يبق لظهورها وقع ولا مرتبة كما أن المنصب الذي يصل إليه الشريف والخسيس فإنه لا يبقى له وقع، ولما اتفق العقلاء على أن ظهور هذه الخوارق منصب عظيم علمنا أنه لابد أن يكون مخصوصا بالأنبياء - عليهم السلام. الحجة الخامسة : لو ظهرت الكرامات على إنسان ثم ادعى ذلك الإنسان على أحد مالا فإما أن يطالبه بالبينة أو لا يطالب لا جائز أن يطالبه ؛ لأنه بتلك الكرامة كمال حاله ودرجته عند الله ومتى كان كذلك امتنع أن يكون في ادعائه المال على الغير كاذبًا، ولا يجوز أن لا يطالب بالبينة لقوله الا الله : «البينة على المدعي واليمين على من أنكر» ولما بطل القسمان علمنا أنه لا يجوز ظهور الكرامة.
والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى: أن منكر الكرامة يلزمه ذلك الإشكال لأنه أثبت للعالم فاعلا محتارًا، فلعل ذلك الفاعل فعل هذه الأمور من قلب الجبال ذهبًا وقلب البحار دمًا وما يشبهه إما من غير رعاية مصلحة - عند من لا يوقف فاعلية الله تعالى على رعاية المصلحة - أو لمصلحة لا نعرفها نحن - عند من يوقف فاعلية الله تعالى على رعاية المصالح. وأما من ينكر الفاعل المختار فيلزمه هذا الاحتمال أيضًا لأن سبب حدوث الحوادث في هذا العالم بتشكيلات فلكية واتصالات كوكبية - على مذهبه ـ ولا يبعد أيضًا أن يحدث في الفك تشكل غريب يوجب حدوث هذه الحوادث في هذا العالم فثبت أن هذا الإشكال لازم على كل الفرق.
والجواب عن الشبهة الثانية : أن الكرامات والمعجزات - وإن اشتركت في كون كل واحد منهما أمرا خارقا للعادة - ولكن تمتاز المعجزة عن الكرامة من وجوه : أحدها: أن الدعوى شرط في النبوة وليست شرطا في الكرامة. وثانيها : أن الحاصل في النبوة ادعاء النبوة، وفي الكرامة إما أن لا تحصل الدعوى، أو إن حصلت لكنها لا تكون دعوى النبوة بل دعوى الولاية. ثالثها: أن المعجزة لا تكون لها معارضة والكرامة قد تكون لها معارضة. والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة أن هذا الذي ألزمتموه علينا لازم عليكم في المعجزات، وذلك لأنه تعالى إذا بعث جمعًا كثيرًا من الأنبياء فظهر على يد كل واحد منهم فعل خارق للعادة فحينئذ تصير الخوارق للعادات موافقة للعادات، وذلك يقدح في كون المعجزة معجزة وإن قلتم : إن تكثير المعجزات إنما يجوز بشرط أن لا يصير انخراق العادة عادة كان هذا هو جوابنا في الكرامات.
والجواب عن الشبهة الرابعة أن دليل النبوة ليس هو الفعل الخارق للعادة فقط، بل هو الفعل مقرونا بدعوى النبوة مع عدم المعارضة، وهذا المجموع لا يحصل لغير الأنبياء، فلا يلزم سقوط وقوعها.
والجواب عن الشبهة الخامسة : أن ظهور الكرامات عليه لا يوجب كونه صادقا في جميع الأمور، فلا جرم يجوز مطالبته بالبينة، وبالله التوفيق.
The fourth argument: If these extraordinary phenomena (khawāriq) appeared upon a prophet and upon someone else, then their occurrence would have no significance or rank, just as the position which reaches the noble and the ignoble has no significance. And since the rational people agree that the appearance of these extraordinary phenomena is a great rank, we know that it must be specific to the prophets (peace be upon them). The fifth argument: If karamāt appeared upon a person, then that person claimed against someone property, either he demands evidence from him or he does not. It is not permissible that he demand evidence from him, because by that karāma his state and rank with God are perfect, and when that is the case, it is impossible that he be lying in his claim of property against another. And it is not permissible that he not demand evidence because of the saying of God (or the Prophet, salAllahu alayhi wasallam): 'Evidence is on the claimant and the oath is on the denier.' And since both divisions are invalid, we know that the appearance of karāma is not permissible. The answer to the first objection (shubha): That the denier of karāma is forced into that difficulty because he affirmed for the world a free-acting agent. So perhaps that agent did these things – turning mountains into gold and seas into blood and the like – either without regard to benefit (according to the one who does not condition God's agency on regard for benefit) or for a benefit we do not know (according to the one who conditions God's agency on regard for benefits). As for the one who denies the free-acting agent, this probability also applies to him because the cause of occurrences of events in this world, according to his doctrine, is through celestial configurations and planetary conjunctions, and it is not unlikely that a strange configuration occurs in the sphere that necessitates the occurrence of these events in this world. So it is established that this difficulty applies to all sects. The answer to the second objection: That karamāt and miracles (mu‘jizāt) – although they share in that each one is an extraordinary matter – yet the miracle is distinguished from karāma in several respects: First: that the claim (da‘wā) is a condition in prophethood and is not a condition in karāma. Second: that what occurs in prophethood is the claim of prophethood, while in karāma either no claim occurs, or if it occurs, it is not a claim of prophethood but a claim of sainthood (wilāya). Third: that the miracle has no counterpart challenging it, while karāma may have a counterpart challenging it. The answer to the third objection: That this which you have required of us is required of you regarding miracles. Because if God sends a large group of prophets and upon the hand of each one an extraordinary act appears, then the extraordinary acts become conforming to custom (normal), and that detracts from the miracle being a miracle. And if you say: the multiplication of miracles is permissible only on condition that the breaking of custom does not become customary, then this is our answer regarding karamāt. The answer to the fourth objection: That the proof of prophethood is not merely the extraordinary act, but rather the act accompanied by the claim of prophethood with the absence of a challenge. And this combination does not occur for non-prophets, so the fall of veneration does not follow. The answer to the fifth objection: That the appearance of karamāt upon him does not necessitate his truthfulness in all matters, so certainly it is permissible to demand evidence from him. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of objections against the possibility of karamāt (miracles of saints). Imam Razi presents two objections (the fourth and fifth) and then gives answers to five objections (shubahāt), including the first three whose statements are not included in this window. The fourth objection argues that if extraordinary acts appear on both prophets and non-prophets, they lose their significance—just as a rank attained by both noble and ignoble loses its value. Since rational people agree such acts are a great rank, they must be exclusive to prophets. The fifth objection argues that if karamāt appear on a saint and he then claims property from someone, either he is asked for evidence or not. Asking for evidence is impermissible because his karāma shows his perfect state with God, making it impossible for him to lie. Not asking is impermissible due to the prophetic hadith 'evidence is on the claimant'. Thus karamāt cannot occur. Razi then answers each objection. The answer to the first objection states that both theists and atheists face the same difficulty: a free agent (God) could create such acts for unknown reasons, and naturalists could attribute them to rare celestial configurations. So the objection is not specific to karamāt. The answer to the second objection distinguishes miracles from karamāt in three ways: (1) the claim of prophethood is a condition for miracles but not for karamāt; (2) in prophethood the claim is of prophethood, while in karamāt it is either no claim or a claim of sainthood; (3) miracles have no challenge (mu‘āraḍa), while karamāt may have a challenge. The answer to the third objection turns the tables: if many prophets appear with miracles, the extraordinary becomes ordinary, which would also invalidate miracles—but the defenders of miracles allow it on condition that the breaking of custom does not become customary, and that same condition applies to karamāt. The answer to the fourth objection states that the proof of prophethood is not just the extraordinary act but the act combined with the claim of prophethood and absence of challenge—a combination unique to prophets. The answer to the fifth objection simply affirms that a saint's karāma does not guarantee his truthfulness in all matters, so demanding evidence is permissible.

Key Terms
خارق للعادة
نبوة
دعوى
معارضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that if saints can perform miracles, then prophethood loses its uniqueness.
Correction: The uniqueness of prophethood lies in the combination of the extraordinary act with the claim of prophethood and the absence of challenge, not the act alone.

Checkpoint
What does Imam Razi say is the proof of prophethood?
A) The extraordinary act alone
B) The extraordinary act accompanied by the claim of prophethood and absence of challenge
C) The extraordinary act accompanied by the claim of sainthood
D) The extraordinary act without any claim

Key Takeaway
The proof of prophethood is the extraordinary act combined with the claim of prophethood and absence of challenge, a combination unique to prophets.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that your faith in the Prophet ﷺ relies more on the awe of miracles than on the complete proof of his prophethood? What would it take for you to internalize the logical combination of claim, act, and challenge as the foundation of your certainty?
?
If someone questioned why the righteous awliya' perform miracles but are not prophets, would you be able to defend the uniqueness of prophethood using Razi's argument? How prepared are you to give that answer from your current understanding?
?
Reflect on your own spiritual journey: do you seek signs that validate your beliefs, or do you seek the full evidence package that the scholars have laid out? What change in your approach might make your conviction more intellectually unshakeable?

The Fifth Objection: Karamat Would Undermine Legal Procedure
Fifth objection and its refutation by Imam Razi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the fifth objection against karamat (conflict with legal procedure) is invalid because a saint's karama does not guarantee truthfulness in all matters.

Core Idea
Karama does not make a saint infallible in every claim; evidence is still required.

The fifth objection argues that if a saint has a karama, he cannot lie, so demanding evidence from him is unnecessary—yet Islamic law requires evidence. Imam Razi's answer is simple: a saint's karama does not guarantee he is truthful in all matters, so he can be asked for evidence just like anyone else.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the fifth objection. It presents a dilemma: either the saint is asked for evidence or not. Both options are problematic according to the objector.
2
Understand the dilemma
The objector claims asking for evidence is wrong because the karama proves he is truthful; not asking is wrong because Islamic law demands evidence.
The objector concludes that karamat cannot be permissible.
3
Study the answer
Razi's answer is that the karama does not guarantee truthfulness in all matters, so asking for evidence is perfectly valid.
This breaks the dilemma.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : لو ظهرت هذه الخوارق على النبي وعلى غيره لم يبق لظهورها وقع ولا مرتبة كما أن المنصب الذي يصل إليه الشريف والخسيس فإنه لا يبقى له وقع، ولما اتفق العقلاء على أن ظهور هذه الخوارق منصب عظيم علمنا أنه لابد أن يكون مخصوصا بالأنبياء - عليهم السلام. الحجة الخامسة : لو ظهرت الكرامات على إنسان ثم ادعى ذلك الإنسان على أحد مالا فإما أن يطالبه بالبينة أو لا يطالب لا جائز أن يطالبه ؛ لأنه بتلك الكرامة كمال حاله ودرجته عند الله ومتى كان كذلك امتنع أن يكون في ادعائه المال على الغير كاذبًا، ولا يجوز أن لا يطالب بالبينة لقوله الا الله : «البينة على المدعي واليمين على من أنكر» ولما بطل القسمان علمنا أنه لا يجوز ظهور الكرامة.
والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى: أن منكر الكرامة يلزمه ذلك الإشكال لأنه أثبت للعالم فاعلا محتارًا، فلعل ذلك الفاعل فعل هذه الأمور من قلب الجبال ذهبًا وقلب البحار دمًا وما يشبهه إما من غير رعاية مصلحة - عند من لا يوقف فاعلية الله تعالى على رعاية المصلحة - أو لمصلحة لا نعرفها نحن - عند من يوقف فاعلية الله تعالى على رعاية المصالح. وأما من ينكر الفاعل المختار فيلزمه هذا الاحتمال أيضًا لأن سبب حدوث الحوادث في هذا العالم بتشكيلات فلكية واتصالات كوكبية - على مذهبه ـ ولا يبعد أيضًا أن يحدث في الفك تشكل غريب يوجب حدوث هذه الحوادث في هذا العالم فثبت أن هذا الإشكال لازم على كل الفرق.
والجواب عن الشبهة الثانية : أن الكرامات والمعجزات - وإن اشتركت في كون كل واحد منهما أمرا خارقا للعادة - ولكن تمتاز المعجزة عن الكرامة من وجوه : أحدها: أن الدعوى شرط في النبوة وليست شرطا في الكرامة. وثانيها : أن الحاصل في النبوة ادعاء النبوة، وفي الكرامة إما أن لا تحصل الدعوى، أو إن حصلت لكنها لا تكون دعوى النبوة بل دعوى الولاية. ثالثها: أن المعجزة لا تكون لها معارضة والكرامة قد تكون لها معارضة. والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة أن هذا الذي ألزمتموه علينا لازم عليكم في المعجزات، وذلك لأنه تعالى إذا بعث جمعًا كثيرًا من الأنبياء فظهر على يد كل واحد منهم فعل خارق للعادة فحينئذ تصير الخوارق للعادات موافقة للعادات، وذلك يقدح في كون المعجزة معجزة وإن قلتم : إن تكثير المعجزات إنما يجوز بشرط أن لا يصير انخراق العادة عادة كان هذا هو جوابنا في الكرامات.
والجواب عن الشبهة الرابعة أن دليل النبوة ليس هو الفعل الخارق للعادة فقط، بل هو الفعل مقرونا بدعوى النبوة مع عدم المعارضة، وهذا المجموع لا يحصل لغير الأنبياء، فلا يلزم سقوط وقوعها.
والجواب عن الشبهة الخامسة : أن ظهور الكرامات عليه لا يوجب كونه صادقا في جميع الأمور، فلا جرم يجوز مطالبته بالبينة، وبالله التوفيق.
The fourth argument: If these extraordinary phenomena (khawāriq) appeared upon a prophet and upon someone else, then their occurrence would have no significance or rank, just as the position which reaches the noble and the ignoble has no significance. And since the rational people agree that the appearance of these extraordinary phenomena is a great rank, we know that it must be specific to the prophets (peace be upon them). The fifth argument: If karamāt appeared upon a person, then that person claimed against someone property, either he demands evidence from him or he does not. It is not permissible that he demand evidence from him, because by that karāma his state and rank with God are perfect, and when that is the case, it is impossible that he be lying in his claim of property against another. And it is not permissible that he not demand evidence because of the saying of God (or the Prophet, salAllahu alayhi wasallam): 'Evidence is on the claimant and the oath is on the denier.' And since both divisions are invalid, we know that the appearance of karāma is not permissible. The answer to the first objection (shubha): That the denier of karāma is forced into that difficulty because he affirmed for the world a free-acting agent. So perhaps that agent did these things – turning mountains into gold and seas into blood and the like – either without regard to benefit (according to the one who does not condition God's agency on regard for benefit) or for a benefit we do not know (according to the one who conditions God's agency on regard for benefits). As for the one who denies the free-acting agent, this probability also applies to him because the cause of occurrences of events in this world, according to his doctrine, is through celestial configurations and planetary conjunctions, and it is not unlikely that a strange configuration occurs in the sphere that necessitates the occurrence of these events in this world. So it is established that this difficulty applies to all sects. The answer to the second objection: That karamāt and miracles (mu‘jizāt) – although they share in that each one is an extraordinary matter – yet the miracle is distinguished from karāma in several respects: First: that the claim (da‘wā) is a condition in prophethood and is not a condition in karāma. Second: that what occurs in prophethood is the claim of prophethood, while in karāma either no claim occurs, or if it occurs, it is not a claim of prophethood but a claim of sainthood (wilāya). Third: that the miracle has no counterpart challenging it, while karāma may have a counterpart challenging it. The answer to the third objection: That this which you have required of us is required of you regarding miracles. Because if God sends a large group of prophets and upon the hand of each one an extraordinary act appears, then the extraordinary acts become conforming to custom (normal), and that detracts from the miracle being a miracle. And if you say: the multiplication of miracles is permissible only on condition that the breaking of custom does not become customary, then this is our answer regarding karamāt. The answer to the fourth objection: That the proof of prophethood is not merely the extraordinary act, but rather the act accompanied by the claim of prophethood with the absence of a challenge. And this combination does not occur for non-prophets, so the fall of veneration does not follow. The answer to the fifth objection: That the appearance of karamāt upon him does not necessitate his truthfulness in all matters, so certainly it is permissible to demand evidence from him. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of objections against the possibility of karamāt (miracles of saints). Imam Razi presents two objections (the fourth and fifth) and then gives answers to five objections (shubahāt), including the first three whose statements are not included in this window. The fourth objection argues that if extraordinary acts appear on both prophets and non-prophets, they lose their significance—just as a rank attained by both noble and ignoble loses its value. Since rational people agree such acts are a great rank, they must be exclusive to prophets. The fifth objection argues that if karamāt appear on a saint and he then claims property from someone, either he is asked for evidence or not. Asking for evidence is impermissible because his karāma shows his perfect state with God, making it impossible for him to lie. Not asking is impermissible due to the prophetic hadith 'evidence is on the claimant'. Thus karamāt cannot occur. Razi then answers each objection. The answer to the first objection states that both theists and atheists face the same difficulty: a free agent (God) could create such acts for unknown reasons, and naturalists could attribute them to rare celestial configurations. So the objection is not specific to karamāt. The answer to the second objection distinguishes miracles from karamāt in three ways: (1) the claim of prophethood is a condition for miracles but not for karamāt; (2) in prophethood the claim is of prophethood, while in karamāt it is either no claim or a claim of sainthood; (3) miracles have no challenge (mu‘āraḍa), while karamāt may have a challenge. The answer to the third objection turns the tables: if many prophets appear with miracles, the extraordinary becomes ordinary, which would also invalidate miracles—but the defenders of miracles allow it on condition that the breaking of custom does not become customary, and that same condition applies to karamāt. The answer to the fourth objection states that the proof of prophethood is not just the extraordinary act but the act combined with the claim of prophethood and absence of challenge—a combination unique to prophets. The answer to the fifth objection simply affirms that a saint's karāma does not guarantee his truthfulness in all matters, so demanding evidence is permissible.

Key Terms
كرامة
بينة
مدعي
منكر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that a saint who performs miracles is infallible in all statements and legal claims.
Correction: A saint's karama indicates spiritual rank but does not guarantee truthfulness in every claim; evidence is still required by Islamic law.

Checkpoint
What is Imam Razi's answer to the fifth objection?
A) The saint is truthful in all matters, so no evidence is needed.
B) The karama does not guarantee truthfulness in all matters, so evidence can be demanded.
C) The objection is invalid because karamat never occur.
D) The saint must always be believed without evidence.

Key Takeaway
A saint's karama does not guarantee his truthfulness in every claim, so the requirement of evidence in legal disputes remains valid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that someone's piety or spiritual status automatically makes their word trustworthy in worldly disputes? How does this lesson challenge that assumption in your own life?
?
Think of a situation where you were tempted to skip proper procedure because of someone's religious reputation. What would it cost you to demand evidence anyway?
?
If a saint entered your local mosque and claimed ownership of the building, what would you do? Would the saint's karamat silence your sense of justice? Why or why not?

Distinguishing Miracle from Karama and Addressing Multiplication Concerns
Answer to second and third objections with distinctions and conditional response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three distinctions between miracles (mu‘jizāt) and karamāt, and how the objection about multiplication of extraordinary acts is answered via a conditional condition.

Core Idea
Miracles are defined by claim of prophethood and no challenge; karamat lack one or both.

In answering the second and third objections, Imam Razi provides clear criteria to distinguish miracles from karamat: (1) the claim of prophethood is a condition for miracles but not for karamat; (2) in karamat the claim is either absent or of sainthood; (3) miracles have no challenge, while karamat may have. He also responds to the third objection about multiplication by noting that a condition must prevent the extraordinary from becoming ordinary.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer to the second objection
Read the three distinctions between miracles and karamat. Notice each distinction focuses on a different aspect: claim, type of claim, and challenge.
These distinctions are foundational for the theology of karamat.
2
Understand the third objection's answer
Read the answer to the third objection. It uses a conditional: if multiplying miracles is allowed as long as the extraordinary does not become customary, then the same condition applies to karamat.
This is a rebuttal by parity of reasoning.
3
Compare the two answers
Notice how both answers protect the uniqueness of prophethood while allowing karamat under specific conditions.

Answer to the Fourth Doubt: The Proof of Prophethood
Al-Razi specifies the criteria for a miracle proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the occurrence of karamat (saintly wonders) does not invalidate the proof of prophethood, because the proof is not merely the extraordinary act but its combination with the claim of prophethood and absence of counter-evidence.

Core Idea
Prophetic proof is the act plus claim plus no counter-evidence.

Al-Razi addresses a common objection: if saints can perform wonders (karamat), how can miracles serve as proofs of prophethood? His answer refines what constitutes a true prophetic proof, showing that karamat lack the crucial combination that makes a miracle decisive.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the fourth doubt
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Focus on al-Razi's three-part condition.
The passage is compact; every phrase matters.
2
Identify the three components of prophetic proof
Note that al-Razi lists: (1) the extraordinary act, (2) the claim of prophethood, (3) no countervailing evidence. This combination is unique to prophets.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: Why does this prevent karamat from being mistaken for prophethood? Because saints lack the claim of prophethood or face counter-evidence (e.g., they may be truthful but not prophets).
This protects the distinctiveness of miracles.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الرابعة أن دليل النبوة ليس هو الفعل الخارق للعادة فقط، بل هو الفعل مقرونا بدعوى النبوة مع عدم المعارضة، وهذا المجموع لا يحصل لغير الأنبياء، فلا يلزم سقوط وقوعها. والجواب عن الشبهة الخامسة : أن ظهور الكرامات عليه لا يوجب كونه صادقا في جميع الأمور، فلا جرم يجوز مطالبته بالبينة، وبالله التوفيق.
And the answer to the fourth doubt is that the proof of prophethood is not merely the extraordinary act, but rather the act accompanied by the claim of prophethood with no countervailing evidence, and this total does not occur for other than prophets, so it is not necessary that its occurrence be nullified. And the answer to the fifth doubt: that the appearance of karamāt (wonders) upon him does not necessitate that he be truthful in all matters, so undoubtedly it is permissible to demand proof from him, and with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage contains al-Razi's answers to two doubts (shubuhāt) raised against the validity of karamāt (extraordinary acts performed by saints) in the context of proving prophethood. The fourth doubt likely argued that if karamāt occur, miracles cease to be distinctive proofs. Al-Razi answers by clarifying that the proof of prophethood is not the extraordinary act alone, but the act combined with the claim of prophethood and the absence of any counteracting evidence. This combination is exclusive to prophets, so karamāt do not undermine the proof. The fifth doubt suggested that karamāt might imply the saint is truthful in all matters, including false claims. Al-Razi answers that karamāt do not entail universal truthfulness; the saint may still be required to produce evidence (bayyinah) for any claim. Thus, the integrity of prophethood is preserved.

Key Terms
دليل النبوة
الفعل الخارق للعادة
دعوى النبوة
المعارضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles lose their probative value if saints can perform similar acts.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that the proof is not the act alone but the act plus claim and no counter-evidence, a combination unique to prophets.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, what makes an extraordinary act a proof of prophethood?
A) The act alone.
B) The act plus the claim of prophethood.
C) The act plus the claim of prophethood and absence of counter-evidence.
D) The act plus the absence of counter-evidence.

Key Takeaway
A prophetic miracle is defined by the combination of the extraordinary act, the claim, and no counter-evidence, ensuring karamat do not threaten the uniqueness of prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does the fact that prophethood is proven by a threefold test (act, claim, no counter-evidence) challenge the way you evaluate claims of religious authority today? Will you start applying this standard to those who claim to speak for God?
?
Al-Razi insists that the act alone is not enough. When have you been impressed by something extraordinary in your spiritual life—a powerful sermon, a seeming miracle—and assumed it validated the person's message without checking their claim? What would it cost you to examine that claim more closely?
?
The condition of 'no counter-evidence' means that a true prophet's proof must be unchallengeable. Is your own faith such that it can withstand any counterarguments? If not, what would it take for you to build that kind of certainty? Are you willing to seek the evidence, even if it unsettles you?

Miracles
intermediate
4 min
Answer to the Fifth Doubt: Karamat Do Not Imply Universal Truthfulness
Al-Razi limits the implications of karamat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the occurrence of karamat does not obligate belief in a saint's truthfulness in all matters, so they may still be required to provide evidence for their claims.

Core Idea
Karamat do not make a saint automatically truthful in all claims.

The fifth doubt suggests that if a saint performs wonders, it might be taken as a sign that everything they say is true, potentially leading to false claims of prophethood. Al-Razi clarifies that karamat only confirm the saint's piety, not universal veracity, so evidence (bayyinah) is still required for any claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the fifth doubt
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice al-Razi's clear rebuttal: karamat do not necessitate truthfulness in all matters.
2
Understand the logical point
Al-Razi says that witnessing a karamat does not make the person 'sadiq' (truthful) in everything. Therefore, if they claim prophethood, they need separate evidence.
This preserves the role of the prophetic challenge (tahaddi).
3
Connect to the broader argument
Reflect on how this answers the doubt: karamat are not a blanket endorsement. The saint is still subject to scrutiny regarding any claim.

Checkpoint
What does al-Razi say about the implications of karamat for a saint's truthfulness?
A) Karamat prove the saint is truthful in everything.
B) Karamat only prove the saint is truthful in religious matters.
C) Karamat do not necessitate that the saint be truthful in all matters.
D) Karamat prove the saint is a prophet.

Key Takeaway
Karamat confirm a saint's piety but do not imply universal truthfulness, so any claim still requires evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted someone’s spiritual claim without question simply because they appeared pious or performed acts you considered miraculous? How does this lesson call you to demand proof—even from those you admire?
?
In your own life, do you assume that because you feel blessed or have experienced what seems like divine favor, everything you believe or do must be correct? What would it look like for you to apply the principle of 'demanding proof' to your own assumptions?
?
When you encounter a teacher or leader whose outward signs of piety impress you, are you willing to test their statements against the evidence of the Qur’an and Sunnah? Or do you let the karāmāt cloud your judgment? What will you change today?
